Refuting the Analogy of Vision with Other Senses
Imam Ashari responds to objections comparing vision to touch, taste, and smell.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari refutes the objection that vision should not entail originatedness in Allah because touch, taste, and smell do not.

Core Idea
Touch, taste, and smell involve material contact and change; vision does not.

In this passage, Imam Ashari addresses an objection raised against his proof for the divine attribute of vision. The objectors claim that senses like touch, taste, and smell do not entail originatedness (ḥadath) in Allah, so why should vision? Ashari provides two lines of response from his school, clarifying the nature of these senses and the permissibility of attributing perception to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection and Responses
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the objectors' argument, then two responses from Ashari's companions.
The objectors assume an analogy between vision and the other senses.
2
Understand the First Response: Physical Contact
The first group of companions argues that touch, taste, and smell involve physical contact (mumāsāt), which necessarily entails change (ḥudūth) in the touching things. This would be impossible for Allah.
This response shows that the analogy fails because the other senses are materially conditioned.
3
Understand the Second Response: Two Intentions
The second group distinguishes between perception created in us (without change in Allah) and a meaning occurring in Allah. Only the former is permissible. The naming of these perceptions is up to divine command.
This response clarifies that the objector may intend either meaning, and only one is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عارضونا : بأن اللمس والذوق والشم ليس فيها إثباتُ الحَدثِ ولا حُدوثُ معنى في البارِي -تعالى-. قيلَ لَهُم : قد قال بعضُ أصحابنا : إنَّ اللَّمسَ ضربٌ مِن ضُروبِ المماسات، وكذلك الذوقُ : وهو اتصالُ اللّسانِ والشَّهَوَاتِ بالجسمِ الذي لهُ الطَّعْمُ ، وإن الشَّمَّ : هو اتصال الخيشوم بالمشْمُومِ، الذي يكونُ عنده الإدراك له، وأنَّ المُتَمَاسَّيْنِ إِنما يَتَمَاسَّانِ بِحُدُوثِ فيهما، وإن في إثباتِ ذلك إثبات حدوث معنى في الباري. ومن أصحابنا مَن يقولُ : لا يخلو القائل أن يكون : أراد بذكره اللمس والذوق والشم، أن يُحدثَ اللَّهُ -تعالى- له إدراكًا في هذه الجوارح، من غير أن يَحدُثَ فيه معنى . أو يكون أراد حدوث معنى فيه . فإن كان أرادَ حُدُوثَ معنّى فيهِ ، فذلك ما لا يَجوزُ، وإن كان أراد حدوث إدراك فينا ، فذلكَ جَائِز ، والأمرُ في التّسمية إلى الله -تعالى- ؛ إِنْ أَمَرَنا أن نسميه لمسا وذوقا وشمَّا سَمَّيْنَاهُ، وإن مَنَعَنَا امْتَنَعْنا .
If they object: that touch, taste, and smell do not entail establishing originatedness (al-ḥadath) nor occurrence of a meaning (ḥudūth ma'nan) in the Eternal, may He be exalted. It is said to them: Some of our companions have said: Touch is a type of contact (mumāsāt), and likewise taste: it is the connection of the tongue and appetites with the body that possesses the flavor, and smell: it is the connection of the nasal passage with the smelled object, at which perception occurs for it, and the two touching things only touch through an occurrence in them, and in establishing that is an establishment of occurrence of a meaning in the Eternal. And some of our companions say: The speaker must be either: intending by his mention of touch, taste, and smell, that Allah, exalted, creates for him perception in these limbs, without a meaning occurring in Him. Or intending the occurrence of a meaning in Him. If he intended the occurrence of a meaning in Him, that is not permissible. If he intended the occurrence of perception in us, that is permissible. And the matter of naming is up to Allah, exalted: if He commands us to call it touch, taste, and smell, we call it; if He prohibits us, we refrain.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objectors argue that touch, taste, and smell do not entail originatedness (ḥadath) or occurrence of a meaning (ḥudūth ma'nan) in Allah, so why should vision be different? The Imam first reports two positions from his companions. The first group argues that these senses involve physical contact (mumāsāt), which necessarily entails occurrence of a meaning (i.e., change) in the perceiver (since contact involves change in both touching things). Therefore, if we try to apply that to Allah, it would imply change in Him, which is impossible. The second group distinguishes between two intentions: if the objector means that Allah creates perception in us without any new meaning in Him, that is permissible; if they mean that a meaning occurs in Allah Himself, that is invalid. Finally, the Imam states that the naming of these perceptions as touch, taste, and smell is subject to divine command; we call them so only if Allah commands us.

Key Terms
اللمس (al-lams)
الذوق (al-dhawq)
الشم (al-sham)
المماسة (al-mumāsāt)
الحدث (al-ḥadath)
حُدوث معنى (ḥudūth ma'nā)
الإدراك (al-idrāk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The senses of touch, taste, and smell are analogous to vision in that they do not entail any change in the perceiver.
Correction: These senses involve physical contact and thus require a change (ḥudūth) in the perceiver, unlike vision which is not materially conditioned.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the first response of the companions rejects the analogy with touch?
A) Touch is not a real sense.
B) Touch involves contact which entails change in both touching things.
C) Touch is forbidden in Islamic law.
D) Touch is not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Imam Ashari refutes the analogy of vision with touch, taste, and smell by showing that these senses involve physical contact and change, which cannot apply to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself explaining Allah's attributes using analogies from your own experience? How does this lesson challenge you to submit to divine commands even when you cannot fully understand?
?
The scholars in this passage debated the nature of perception with humility, acknowledging that naming is up to Allah. In what area of your faith are you tempted to insist on your own reasoning rather than accepting what Allah has revealed?
?
If Allah were to command you to call something by a name that goes against your natural understanding, would you obey? Reflect on a time when your heart resisted a clear command. How can you train yourself to say 'we call it only if He commands'?

Hearing Allah's Speech: The Ash'ari Consensus
Imam Ash'ari states the Ash'ari consensus on hearing Allah's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the permissibility of hearing Allah's speech and the scriptural evidence from the story of Prophet Musa (AS).

Core Idea
Allah can make His servants hear His speech without physical contact.

Imam Al-Ash'ari presents the unanimous view of his school regarding the attribute of hearing (sam'). The companions agreed that it is possible for Allah to make His creation hear His speech directly. This is supported by the event of Prophet Moses hearing Allah at Mount Sinai.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the structure of the statement.
The passage is concise; focus on the consensus and the example.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the Arabic terms for hearing (السمع) and speech (التكلم). Understand how they relate to the attribute of Allah.
The term 'سمع' here refers to the act of hearing, not the attribute of hearing.
3
Reflect on the significance of the example
The example of Prophet Musa (AS) shows that hearing Allah's speech is possible without physical contact, thus avoiding objections of anthropomorphism.
Compare this with previous discussions on vision and touch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السمع، فلم يختلف أصحابنا فيه، وجَوَّزُوا جميعا ؛ وقالوا : إنه جائز أن يُسمِعَنَا البارِي تَعالَى - نفسَهُ مُتَكلّما ، وقد أسْمَعَ موسَى عليهِ السَّلامُ - نَفْسَهُ مُتَكَلِّمًا .
As for hearing, our companions did not differ concerning it, and they all permitted it; and they said: It is permissible that the Eternal, exalted, make us hear Himself speaking, and He made Moses, peace be upon him, hear Himself speaking.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage notes the unanimous agreement of the Ash'ari companions on the permissibility of hearing (sam'). They all permitted that Allah can make His servants hear His speech (kalam) directly, without any created medium. The example given is that Allah made Prophet Moses hear Himself speaking at Mount Sinai. This is distinct from the previous discussion on touch and vision because hearing does not involve physical contact (mumāsāt), so it is free from the objection of change in the Divine.

Key Terms
السمع (al-sam')
التكلم (al-takallum)
موسى عليه السلام (Musa AS)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what example is given for Allah making Himself heard?
A) Adam
B) Moses
C) Muhammad
D) The angels

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school unanimously holds that Allah can make His speech heard directly, as demonstrated by His speaking to Moses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah can speak to you directly through His words in the Qur'an, or do you treat it as merely a historical text? How would your life change if you listened to Allah's speech as Musa did?
?
Have you ever felt that Allah is distant and silent? Reflect on the fact that He made Moses hear His speech directly. What does that tell you about His desire to communicate with His servants? Will you commit to seeking His speech in the Qur'an with the intention of hearing Him speak to you today?
?
If hearing Allah's speech is possible and He has spoken to prophets, why do you not feel the urgency to listen when the Qur'an is recited? What needs to change in your heart so that you become one who truly hears Allah's speech?

Quranic Proof for Seeing Allah and Refuting Alternative Interpretations
Imam Ashari cites the Quranic verse on vision and refutes interpretations as consideration or mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Ashari uses the verse from Surah al-Qiyama as evidence for the vision of Allah and why alternative interpretations are invalid.

Core Idea
The Hereafter is not a realm of consideration, so 'looking at their Lord' must be literal seeing.

Imam Ashari now turns to the strongest scriptural evidence for the vision of Allah in the afterlife: the verse from Surah al-Qiyama. But the word 'looking' (nāzira) has been interpreted in other ways. In this passage, he cites the verse and then systematically refutes two alternative readings, showing why only the literal meaning is valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its translation
Notice the verse: 'Faces that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord.' This is the primary textual proof.
2
Identify the first alternative interpretation
Some claim 'looking' means 'considering' like in the camel verse. Ask: why is this invalid?
3
Identify the second alternative interpretation
Others claim it means 'compassionate' like in the verse about God not looking at people. Why is this invalid?
4
Understand the Imam's reasoning
The Hereafter is not a realm of consideration, and the Eternal cannot be object of compassion. Both interpretations lead to absurdities.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Notice how the Imam uses linguistic analysis and rational consistency to defend the literal meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أنَّ اللهَ تعالى - يُرَى بالأبصارِ قوله -تعالى- : وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةُ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ [القيامة : ۲۲، ۲۳]، ولا يجوزُ أن يكونَ مَعْنَى قولِهِ : إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ مُعتَبَرَةٌ ، كقوله : أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الْإِبِلِ كَيْفَ خُلِقَتْ ؛ لأنَّ الآخِرَةَ ليسَتْ بِدارِ اعْتِبارِ . ولَا يَجوزُ أنْ يَعْنِي : مُتَعَطَّفَةً رَاحِمةً، كما قال: ﴿وَلَا يَنظُرُ إِلَيْهِمْ ﴾ [آل عمران : ٧٧] أي: لا يرْحَمُهُمْ ولا يتَعَطَّفٌ عليهِم ؛ لأن الباريَ لا يجوز أن يُتعطف عليه .
And the evidence that Allah, exalted, is seen by the eyes is His statement, exalted: 'Faces that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (Qiyamah 22-23). And it is not permissible that the meaning of His saying 'looking at their Lord' be 'considering' (mu'tabara), like His saying: 'Do they not look at the camels how they are created?' because the Hereafter is not a realm of consideration. And it is not permissible that it mean 'compassionate, merciful', as He said: 'And He does not look at them' (Aal-Imran 77), i.e., He does not have mercy on them and is not compassionate towards them, because it is not permissible that the Eternal be the object of compassion.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the primary scriptural proof for the vision of Allah in the afterlife. The Imam cites the verse from Surah al-Qiyama: 'Faces that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord.' He then refutes two alternative interpretations of 'looking' (nāzira). First, some claim it means 'considering' (mu'tabara), as in the verse 'Do they not look at the camels how they are created?' But this is invalid because the Hereafter is not a realm of consideration (dār i'tibār); it is a place of reward and vision. Second, some claim it means 'compassionate, merciful' (muta'aṭṭifa rāḥima), citing the verse 'He does not look at them' meaning He does not have mercy. But this is also invalid because it would imply that the Eternal (al-Bāri) is the object of compassion, which is not permissible. Thus, the only sound interpretation is the literal meaning of seeing with the eyes (bi-l-absār).

Key Terms
الأبصار (al-absār)
النظر (al-naẓar)
المعنى (al-ma'nā)
الاعتبار (al-i'tibār)
التعطف (al-ta'aṭṭuf)
الرحمة (al-raḥma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'nazar' in the vision verse means 'consideration' or 'mercy'.
Correction: The Hereafter is not a realm of consideration, and the Eternal cannot be object of compassion, so the only valid meaning is literal vision.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Ashari reject the interpretation of 'looking' as 'considering'?
A) Because it is grammatically incorrect
B) Because the Hereafter is not a realm of consideration
C) Because the verse is about vision
D) Because the companions rejected it

Key Takeaway
The literal meaning of 'looking at their Lord' in Surah al-Qiyama is seeing with the eyes, and alternative interpretations are refuted by context and rational consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the faces of the believers are radiant because they are looking at their Lord, is your face radiant now with the hope of that vision? What is one worldly attachment that dims that radiance, and what step will you take today to remove it?
?
Imam Ashari shows that the Hereafter is not a place for consideration, but for direct encounter. Are you living this life as a preparation for that encounter, or are you content with distant speculation? Write down one concrete action you will take this week to increase your longing for Allah's vision.
?
The refutation of 'compassion' reminds us that we cannot project our emotions onto Allah. In what areas of your life do you treat Allah as if He needs your pity or approval? How can you replace that with pure awe and gratitude?

On Attributing Senses to God: Touch, Taste, Smell, and Hearing
Al-Ash'ari clarifies the permissibility of attributing touch, taste, smell, and hearing to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on attributing bodily senses to God, distinguishing between change in God and perception in the creature, and the role of divine command in naming.

Core Idea
God's senses are about His creating perception in us, not change in Him.

Al-Ash'ari addresses a potential confusion: when we say God 'touches' or 'smells,' we might think it implies God acquires new attributes. He clarifies that these expressions refer to God creating acts of perception in created beings, and the naming itself is subject to God's command. He then adds that hearing God's speech is unanimously allowed, as proven by Moses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Al-Ash'ari distinguishes between meanings.
2
Identify the key distinction for touch, taste, smell
Al-Ash'ari presents two interpretations: either a meaning occurs in God (impermissible) or perception occurs in us (permissible). Mark how he resolves the issue of naming by referring to divine command.
3
Understand the unanimous permission for hearing
Al-Ash'ari states that all his companions agreed that hearing God's speech is permissible, citing the example of Moses. Reflect on how this differs from the previous senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجوارح، من غير أن يَحدُثَ فيه معنى. أو يكون أراد حدوث معنى فيه. فإن كان أرادَ حُدُوثَ معنى فيهِ، فذلك ما لا يَجوزُ، وإن كان أراد حدوث إدراك فينا، فذلكَ جَائِز، والأمرُ في التّسمية إلى الله -تعالى-؛ إِنْ أَمَرَنا أن نسميه لمسا وذوقا وشمَّا سَمَّيْنَاهُ، وإن مَنَعَنَا امْتَنَعْنا. وأمَّا السمعُ، فلم يختلف أصحابنا فيه، وجَوَّزُوا جميعا؛ وقالوا: إنه جائز أن يُسْمِعَنَا البارِي تَعالَى - نفسَهُ مُتَكلّما، وقد أسْمَعَ موسَى عليهِ السَّلامُ - نَفْسَهُ مُتَكَلِّمًا.
The limbs, without that a meaning (ma'na) occurs in it. Or he intended that a meaning occurs in it. For if he intended that a meaning occurs in it, that is not permissible. And if he intended that an act of perception (idrak) occurs in us, then that is permissible. And the matter of naming is to God, exalted is He; if He commands us to name it touching (lams), tasting (dhawq), and smelling (shamm), we name it, and if He forbids us, we refrain. As for hearing (sam'), our companions did not differ about it, and they permitted all of it; and they said: It is permissible that the Creator, exalted is He, make us hear His own self speaking, and He made Moses, peace be upon him, hear His own self speaking.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage discusses the attribution of bodily senses—touch, taste, smell, and hearing—to God. Al-Ash'ari begins by clarifying a key distinction: when we say that God 'touches' or 'smells,' we cannot mean that a new attribute or event (ma'na) occurs in God's essence, because that would imply change and temporality, which is impossible for God. Instead, if the expression is used, it must refer to God creating an act of perception (idrak) in the created being (e.g., the sensation of touch in a person). This is permissible because it does not attribute change to God. Furthermore, the actual naming of these acts as 'touch,' 'taste,' or 'smell' is subject to divine authorization—if God commands us to use those terms, we do so; if He prohibits, we refrain. Regarding hearing (sam'), Al-Ash'ari reports that all of his companions (the Ash'arites) agree that it is permissible for God to make us hear His own speech directly. This is proven by the fact that God made Prophet Moses hear His own speech (the event at Mount Sinai). Thus, hearing God's speech without intermediary is possible, but the act of hearing occurs in the creature, not in God's essence.

Key Terms
الجوارح
معنى
إدراك
جائز
لمس
ذوق
شم
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributing senses to God implies that God undergoes change or acquires new attributes.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari clarifies that it means God creates perception in the creature, not that a new attribute occurs in God.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ash'ari, when we say that God 'touches' something, what must we mean?
A) A new attribute occurs in God's essence.
B) God creates an act of perception in the created being.
C) God experiences physical contact.
D) It is metaphorical and has no real meaning.

Key Takeaway
Attributing senses to God refers to His creating perception in the creature, and the naming is subject to divine command; hearing God's speech is permissible as shown by the example of Moses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your ability to hear, touch, taste, and smell is a direct act of God creating perception in you each moment, does that change how you use these senses? What would you do differently today if you truly believed that every sensation is a gift from Him?
?
Al-Ash'ari says naming these acts is subject to God's command. Are there any phrases you have used to describe God that are not supported by revelation? What would it cost you to submit to His authorization in your speech about Him?
?
Moses heard God's speech directly. Do you live with the expectation that God can speak to you—through His Book, through circumstances, or in your heart? What would it look like to cultivate that expectation today, and what fears hold you back?

The Scriptural Foundation and Rejection of Two Interpretations
Al-Ash'ari cites the verse and rejects the first two alternative meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses Quranic evidence and rational reasoning to reject the interpretations of 'taking heed' and 'compassion' for the word 'nazar' in the verse about seeing God on the Day of Judgment.

Core Idea
The Hereafter is a realm of vision, not reflection or compassion.

Al-Ash'ari begins his proof for the beatific vision with a powerful Quranic verse. He then systematically refutes two alternative interpretations that would undermine the literal meaning. This lesson focuses on the first two rejections—'taking heed' and 'compassion'—which are shown to be logically and contextually impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic verse and its English translation. Notice the key word 'ناظرة' (looking).
This verse is central to the Ash'ari proof for seeing God.
2
Understand the first rejected interpretation: 'taking heed'
Al-Ash'ari argues that 'ناظرة' cannot mean 'taking heed' because the Hereafter is not a place for learning lessons.
The Quranic comparison to looking at camels is for this world only.
3
Understand the second rejected interpretation: 'compassion'
He then rejects the meaning 'turning with compassion' because such a meaning would attribute change to God, which is impossible.
The verse 'He will not look at them' means He will not have mercy, but that usage does not apply to God looking at believers.
4
Reflect on why these rejections are valid
Consider why the Hereafter is not a realm of 'taking heed' and why God cannot be described as 'turning with compassion'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليلُ على أنَّ اللهَ تعالى - يُرَى بالأبصارِ قوله -تعالى-: ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةُ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ﴾ [القيامة: 22، 23]، ولا يجوزُ أن يكونَ مَعْنَى قولِهِ: إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ مُعتَبَرَةٌ، كقوله: ﴿ أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الْإِبِلِ كَيْفَ خُلِقَتْ ﴾؛ لأنَّ الآخِرَةَ ليسَتْ بِدارِ اعْتِبارِ. ولا يَجوزُ أنْ يَعْنِي: مُتَعَطَّفَةً رَاحِمةً، كما قال: ﴿ وَلَا يَنظُرُ إِلَيْهِمْ ﴾ [آل عمران: 77] أي: لا يرْحَمُهُمْ ولا يتَعَطَّفٌ عليهِم؛ لأن الباريَ لا يجوز أن يُتعطف عليه. ولا يَجوزُ أن يَعْنِي: مُنتَظِرةً؛ لأنَّ النظر إذا قُرِنَ بذكْرِ الوُجُوهِ لم يكنْ مَعْنَاهُ نَظَرَ القَلْبِ الَّذِي هو انتظار، كَمَا إِذا قُرِنَ النظرُ بذكرِ القلبِ لم يكنْ مَعْناه نَظَرَ العَيْنِ؛ لأنَّ القائل إذا قال: «انظُرْ بِقَلْبِكَ في هذَا الأَمْرِ» كانَ معْناهُ نظرَ القَلبِ، وكَذَلِكَ إِذا قُرِنَ النظرُ بالوَجْهِ لَمْ يكُن معناه إلا نظرَ الوَجْهِ، والنَّظَرُ بالوَجْهِ هو النَّظَرُ الذي هو الرُّؤْيَةُ التي تكونُ بالعَيْنِ التي في الوَجْهِ. فَصَحَّ أن معنى قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ﴾: رَائِيَةٌ، إِذْ لَمْ يَجُزْ أَنْ يَعْني شيئًا مِن وُجوهِ النَّظرِ الأخرى. وإذا كان النظرُ لا يخلو مِنْ وُجوه أربع، وفَسَدَ مِنها ثلاثة أوجه، صح الوجهُ الرَّابِعُ، وهو نظَرُ رُؤْيةِ العَيْنِ التي في الوجهِ. فإن قال قائل: أَلَيسَ قَدْ قالَ اللهُ -تعالى-: ﴿ وَوُجُوهٌ يَوْمَن بَاسِرَةٌ تَظُنُّ أَن يُفْعَلَ بِهَا فَافِرَةٌ ﴾ [القيامة: 24، 25]، والظَّنُّ لا يكونُ له
And the proof that God, exalted is He, will be seen with the eyes is His statement, exalted is He: 'Faces that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' (Qiyamah 75:22-23). And it is not permissible that the meaning of His statement 'looking at their Lord' is 'taking heed,' like His statement: 'Do they not look at the camels, how they are created?' (Ghasiyah 88:17) because the Hereafter is not a realm of taking heed. And it is not permissible that He means 'turning with mercy,' as He said: 'and He will not look at them' (Aal 'Imran 3:77), meaning He will not have mercy on them nor turn to them with compassion, because the Creator cannot be the object of such turning for compassion. And it is not permissible that He means 'waiting' because the word looking (nazar) when connected to the mention of faces cannot have the meaning of the heart's looking which is waiting, just as when looking is connected to the mention of the heart, it cannot have the meaning of the eye's looking. For if someone says: 'Look with your heart at this matter,' it means the heart's looking. Similarly, when looking is connected to the face, its meaning can only be the face's looking, and looking with the face is the looking that is the sight which occurs by the eye that is in the face. Thus it is established that the meaning of His statement, exalted is He: 'looking at their Lord' is 'seeing' (ra'iyah), since it is not permissible that He means any of the other forms of looking. And since looking cannot be free from four aspects, and three aspects are invalid, the fourth aspect is valid, and it is the looking of the sight of the eye that is in the face. So if someone says: Did not God, exalted is He, say: 'And faces that Day will be frowning, expecting that a calamity will befall them' (Qiyamah 75:24-25) and expectation (zann) is not...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents Al-Ash'ari's proof for the beatific vision (ru'ya) of God on the Day of Judgment, using both scriptural and rational arguments. The proof begins with a direct Quranic verse: 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' (Faces that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord). Al-Ash'ari argues that the word 'ناظرة' (looking) here cannot be interpreted in three alternative ways commonly suggested by the Mu'tazila and others, leaving only the literal meaning of sight with the eyes. 

First, it cannot mean 'taking heed' (معتبرة) like the verse 'Do they not look at the camels?' because the Hereafter is not a place for reflection and taking heed; it is a realm of recompense, not a place for learning lessons. 

Second, it cannot mean 'turning with mercy and compassion,' as in the verse 'ولا ينظر إليهم' (He will not look at them) meaning He will not have mercy on them. This interpretation fails because the word 'نظر' when used with 'إلى' and in the context of the face usually indicates visual sight, and also because God cannot be described as undergoing such emotional changes as turning with compassion toward someone. 

Third, it cannot mean 'waiting' (منتظرة) because 'نظر' when associated with the face (وجه) indicates physical sight, just as when it is associated with the heart (قلب) it indicates mental consideration. The phrase 'انظر بقلبك' (look with your heart) clearly means mental reflection, not visual sight. Conversely, when 'نظر' is connected to the face, it can only mean physical sight with the eye located in the face. 

Al-Ash'ari then employs a method of division (sabr wa-taqsim): the word 'نظر' has four possible meanings—taking heed, compassion, waiting, and physical sight. Since the first three are invalid in this context, the fourth (physical sight) must be the intended meaning. The proof concludes that the verse necessarily affirms that the believers will see God with their physical eyes on the Day of Judgment. The final sentence, though cut off, seems to anticipate an objection about the word 'zann' (expectation) in the next verse, but the core proof is complete.

Key Terms
نظر
اعتبار
تعطف
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that 'ناظرة' in the verse means 'taking heed' or 'reflecting', similar to other Quranic uses.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the Hereafter is not a realm for taking heed, so that meaning is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Ash'ari reject the interpretation of 'ناظرة' as 'taking heed'?
A) Because the word 'ناظرة' never means 'taking heed' in Arabic.
B) Because the Hereafter is not a realm of taking heed; it is a realm of recompense.
C) Because the verse about camels shows that 'taking heed' is only for this world.
D) Because the faces are described as 'radiant' which precludes 'taking heed'.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari uses the context of the Hereafter and divine transcendence to rule out 'taking heed' and 'compassion' as meanings of 'nazar' in the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see your Lord in the Hereafter, or is your hope in other rewards? The verse says faces will be radiant looking at Him — does this radiance describe your inner expectation?
?
Al-Ash'ari rejects that the Hereafter is a place for reflection. Are you still treating your life as a mere lesson to be learned, not a preparation for a direct encounter with God?
?
If God cannot be the object of compassion because it would imply change, then your relationship with Him is not based on your eliciting His mercy by your actions — it is purely His gift. How does this affect your sense of dependence on Him?

Rejection of 'Waiting' and the Final Proof by Division
Al-Ash'ari refutes the third interpretation and concludes by sabr wa-taqsim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's linguistic argument that 'nazar' when associated with 'face' must mean physical sight, and his use of the method of division (sabr wa-taqsim) to conclusively prove the beatific vision.

Core Idea
When 'looking' is tied to the face, it can only mean physical sight with the eyes.

Having rejected two interpretations, Al-Ash'ari now tackles the third—'waiting'—using a powerful linguistic analogy. He then employs the logical method of division (sabr wa-taqsim): since 'nazar' has four possible meanings and three are invalid, the fourth—physical sight—must be intended. This lesson completes the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument against 'waiting'
Al-Ash'ari argues that 'ناظرة' cannot mean 'waiting' because the word 'nazar' when connected to 'face' indicates physical sight, just as when connected to 'heart' it indicates mental consideration.
He uses a linguistic rule: the meaning of 'nazar' depends on the organ it is associated with.
2
Understand the linguistic analogy
Compare 'look with your heart' (mental) vs. 'look with your face' (physical). This clarifies that 'nazar' with 'wajh' must mean sight with the eye.
This is a key point in Ash'ari methodology.
3
Follow the sabr wa-taqsim (division)
Al-Ash'ari states that 'nazar' has four possible meanings. Three have been proven invalid, so the fourth—physical sight—is the intended meaning.
This logical method is common in kalam.
4
Conclude the proof
The verse therefore means the believers will see God with their eyes on the Day of Judgment.
The argument is complete even though the last sentence is cut off.

Contextual Pairing: 'Nazar' with 'Wajh' Means Physical Sight
The author refutes an objection that 'nazar' in the afterlife means heart vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the pairing of 'nazar' with 'wajh' in the Quranic verse proves that the vision of Allah in the afterlife is physical sight, not inner vision.

Core Idea
Contextual pairing determines meaning: 'nazar' with 'face' means physical sight.

Imam al-Ash'ari presents a powerful linguistic argument to defend the literal meaning of the verse about seeing Allah. The objector claims 'nazar' means heart-vision, but the author shows that the word's flexibility and its association with 'wajh' require the physical interpretation. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the verse and the objector's claim that 'nazar' here means inner (heart) vision. Notice how the objector relies on a metaphorical interpretation.
The verse is Surah Al-Qiyamah 75:22-23.
2
Analyze the author's refutation
The author compares 'nazar' with 'zann' (opinion) to demonstrate how pairing with a word determines its meaning. 'Zann' is exclusively heart-based, so pairing with 'face' would be odd. But 'nazar' is flexible.
3
Follow the logical necessity
If 'nazar' were only heart-based, pairing it with 'face' would still mean heart-vision. But since it can be physical, pairing with 'face' indicates physical sight. This is a logical deduction from linguistic usage.
The author uses a conditional argument: if exclusive, then it would revert to heart; but it's not exclusive, so it indicates face.
4
Reflect on the principle
This argument exemplifies the classical kalam method: using linguistic context to determine theological meaning. It shows how careful analysis of Quranic language resolves disputes.
This is a key principle in Islamic theology: the apparent meaning is preferred unless there is a compelling reason to interpret metaphorically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَذَلِكَ قَوْلُهُ: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ أَرَادَ نَظَرَ الْقَلْبِ. قِيلَ لَهُ: لَا؛ لِأَنَّ الظَّنَّ لَا يَكُونُ بِالْوَجْهِ، وَلَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِالْقَلْبِ، فَلَمَّا قُرِنَ الظَّنُّ بِذِكْرِ الْوَجْهِ كَانَ مَعْنَاهُ ظَنَّ الْقَلْبِ؛ إِذْ لَمْ يَكُنِ الظَّنُّ إِلَّا بِهِ فَلَوْ كَانَ النَّظَرُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا بِالْقَلْبِ لَوَجَبَ إِذَا ذَكَرَهُ، مَعَ ذِكْرِ الْوَجْهِ، أَن يُرْجَعَ بِهِ إِلَى الْقَلْبِ. فَلَمَّا كَانَ النَّظَرُ قَدْ يَكُونُ بِالْوَجْهِ وَبِغَيْرِهِ، وَجَبَ إِذَا قَرَنَهُ بِذِكْرِ الْوَجْهِ أَن يُرِيدَ بِهِ نَظَرَ الْوَجْهِ، كَمَا أَنَّهُ إِذَا قَرَنَهُ بِذِكْرِ الْقَلْبِ وَجَبَ أَن يُرِيدَ بِهِ نَظَرَ الْقَلْبِ.
And similarly His saying: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' — He meant the vision of the heart. It was said to him: No; because 'opinion' is not with the face, and it is only with the heart. So when 'opinion' was paired with the mention of the face, its meaning was the opinion of the heart, since opinion is only by it. Now if 'sight' were only with the heart, then if He mentioned it along with the mention of the face, it would necessarily be referred back to the heart. But since sight may be with the face or with other than it, it is necessary, when He pairs it with the mention of the face, that He intend by it the sight of the face, just as when He pairs it with the mention of the heart, it is necessary that He intend by it the vision of the heart.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer argument. The objector claims that the Quranic verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' (Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord) refers to inner vision (vision of the heart) rather than physical sight. The author refutes this by analyzing the pairing of words. He points out that 'الظن' (opinion) is exclusively an act of the heart, not the face. When the verse mentions 'ظن' (in context, though not explicitly in the quotation) or associates it with 'وجه' (face), the meaning is heart-based. However, 'النظر' (sight/vision) can be either physical (with the eyes/face) or inner (with the heart). The author argues: If 'نظر' were only inner, then when it appears with 'وجه', it would revert to the heart. But since 'نظر' can be physical, its pairing with 'وجه' indicates physical sight. This is a classical kalam argument based on linguistic usage: the context determines the meaning, and the mention of 'وجه' points to the bodily organ of sight.

Key Terms
نظر
وجه
قلب
ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that 'nazar' in the verse must be metaphorical because God cannot be seen in the afterlife.
Correction: This passage shows that the literal meaning (physical sight) is intended based on the linguistic pairing of 'nazar' with 'wajh' (face).

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that 'nazar' in the verse means inner (heart) vision?
A) Because 'nazar' is never used for the heart in the Quran
B) Because 'nazar' can be physical, and pairing it with 'wajh' indicates physical sight
C) Because the verse explicitly says 'with the eyes'
D) Because the objector did not provide any evidence

Key Takeaway
When a word like 'nazar' can be both physical and inner, pairing it with 'face' indicates physical sight by the face.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Answering the Objection from 'Sights Do Not Perceive Him'
The author answers the objection from the verse denying sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author reconciles the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' with the belief in seeing Allah in the Hereafter by limiting it to this world.

Core Idea
The Quran does not contradict; the verse denying vision applies to this world only.

When faced with a verse that seems to deny any vision of God, we must examine it in light of other clear texts. The author here addresses the apparent conflict by showing that the verse refers specifically to this worldly life. This reasoning preserves both the literal meaning of the verse and the established belief in the beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Read the objection based on the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'. Understand how it challenges the belief in seeing God.
The objector assumes the verse is absolute.
2
Understand the author's answer
The author restricts the verse to this world only, citing the principle that the Quran does not contradict itself.
The other verse affirming vision (إلى ربها ناظرة) is for the Hereafter.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why the Quran's consistency is a decisive argument. No contradiction means the denial must be time-bound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ الْكَلَامِ فِي الرُّؤْيَةِ. فَإِن قَالُوا: فَمَا مَعْنَى قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَلَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾؟ قِيلَ لَهُمْ: فِي الدُّنْيَا دُونَ الْآخِرَةِ؛ لِأَنَّ الْقُرْآنَ لَا يَتَنَاقَضُ. فَلَمَّا قَالَ فِي آيَةٍ أُخْرَى: إِنَّهُ لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ، عَلِمْنَا أَنَّ الْوَقْتَ الَّذِي قَالَ: إِنَّهُ لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ فِيهِ، غَيْرُ الْوَقْتِ الَّذِي أَخْبَرَنَا أَنَّهَا تَنظُرُ إِلَيْهِ فِيهِ. فَإِن قَالَ قَائِلٌ: مَا أَنكَرْتُمْ أَن يَكُونَ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ أَيْ: إِلَى ثَوَابِ رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ؟ قِيلَ لَهُ: ثَوَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى غَيْرُهُ، وَلَا يَجُوزُ أَن يُعْدَلَ بِالْكَلَامِ عَنِ الْحَقِيقَةِ إِلَى الْمَجَازِ بِغَيْرِ حُجَّةٍ وَلَا دِلَالَةٍ.
The chapter on speech about the vision. So if they say: What is the meaning of His saying, Exalted is He: 'Sights do not perceive Him, and He perceives sights'? It is said to them: In this world, not the Hereafter, because the Quran does not contradict. So when He said in another verse that sights do not perceive Him, we knew that the time in which He said that sights do not perceive Him is other than the time in which He informed us that they look at Him. So if a person says: What prevents you from taking His saying, Exalted is He: 'Looking at their Lord' to mean looking at the reward of their Lord? It is said to him: The reward of God, Exalted is He, is other than Him, and it is not permissible to shift speech from the literal to the metaphorical without proof or evidence.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage contains two objections and answers under the heading 'باب الكلام في الرؤية' (Chapter on the Vision of God). The first objection: The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sights do not perceive Him) seems to deny any vision of God. The answer: This refers to this world only, not the Hereafter. The author argues that the Quran does not contradict itself; since another verse affirms vision ('وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة'), the verse about non-perception must be restricted to this world. The second objection: The phrase 'إلى ربها ناظرة' (looking at their Lord) might metaphorically mean looking at God's reward. The answer: This is invalid because God's reward is distinct from God Himself, and moving from literal meaning to metaphorical requires clear evidence, which is absent here. Thus, the literal meaning of seeing God in the Hereafter is upheld.

Key Terms
رؤية
الأبصار
لا تدركه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' absolutely denies any vision of God forever.
Correction: It only denies vision in this world; in the Hereafter, vision is affirmed by other verses.

Checkpoint

A) Because the verse was abrogated
B) Because 'الأبصار' means inner vision, not eyesight
C) Because it refers to this world only
D) Because it is a metaphorical statement

Key Takeaway
The verse denying sight of God refers specifically to this world, not the afterlife.

The author refutes the metaphorical interpretation of the vision verse.

Resolving the Objection: 'No Vision Can Grasp Him' Refers to This World
Al-Ashari answers the objection on the meaning of 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' by specifying this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ashari reconciles the verse denying vision of Allah with other verses affirming it, by specifying the temporal context.

Core Idea
The Qur'an does not contradict itself; the denial of vision applies to this world, not the Hereafter.

In this passage, al-Ashari addresses a key objection raised by those who deny the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter. They cite the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' as absolute. al-Ashari's response is elegant: he shows that the Qur'an itself distinguishes between two different times. Let us see how he does this.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the translation. Notice how the opponents quote the verse and how al-Ashari responds.
The passage is a typical objection-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the key terms
Pay attention to the terms: 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (no vision can grasp Him), 'الدنيا' (this world), 'الآخرة' (the Hereafter). These are central to the argument.
3
Understand the principle of non-contradiction
Al-Ashari states that the Qur'an does not contradict itself. This is the warrant for his interpretation. Reflect on why this principle is important in Islamic theology.
This principle (la tanāquḍ) is a foundational hermeneutic tool.
4
See the temporal distinction
Al-Ashari resolves the apparent contradiction by assigning the denial of vision to this world and the affirmation of vision (from other verses) to the Hereafter. Understand how this harmonizes the verses.
5
Consider the significance
This answer preserves both texts and upholds the doctrine that believers will see Allah in the afterlife. Why is this important for the Ash'ari position?
This is a classic example of tawfiq (reconciliation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله - تعالى -: ﴿وَلَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَرَ﴾؟ قيل لهم : في الدُّنيا دُونَ الآخِرَةِ؛ لأنَّ القرآنَ لا يَتناقَضُ. فلما قال في آية أخرى : إنه لا تدركه الأبصار علمنا أن الوقت، الذي قالَ : إِنه لا تُدرِكُهُ الأبصارُ فيهِ، غَيْرُ الوقت الذي أخبرنا أَنَّهَا تنظُرُ إليهِ فيهِ.
If they say: 'Then what is the meaning of His statement, Exalted is He: {No vision can grasp Him, and He grasps (all) vision}?' It is said to them: 'In this world, not the Hereafter, because the Qur'an does not contradict itself. So when He said in another verse that no vision can grasp Him, we know that the time in which He said that no vision can grasp Him is different from the time in which He informed us that they will look at Him.'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer. The opponents cite the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' to deny the vision of Allah. al-Ashari answers by specifying the temporal context: the verse refers to this world, not the Hereafter. His warrant is that the Qur'an does not contradict itself; since another verse affirms that faces will look at their Lord (in the Hereafter), the two verses must refer to different times. The objection is thus resolved by harmonization without denying either text.

Key Terms
الرؤية
لا تدركه الأبصار
التناقض
الدنيا
الآخرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' is absolute and denies any possibility of seeing Allah in any realm.
Correction: Al-Ashari shows the verse is temporally qualified: it applies only to this world. The Hereafter is a different time where believers will see Allah, as affirmed by other verses.

Checkpoint

B) He interprets 'no vision can grasp Him' as referring to this world, not the Hereafter.
C) He says the verse is metaphorical and does not mean literal vision.
D) He claims the verse was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' applies to this world; in the Hereafter, believers will see Allah.

Refuting the Reward Interpretation of 'Looking at Their Lord'
Al-Ashari refutes a metaphorical interpretation of 'looking at their Lord'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ashari rejects the metaphorical interpretation of 'looking at their Lord' as looking at reward, based on the principle of literal meaning and logical consistency.

Core Idea
Literal meaning cannot be abandoned without proof; otherwise, all scripture becomes unstable.

This passage presents a classic objection against the literal vision of Allah in the Hereafter: perhaps 'looking' means looking at His reward, not His essence. Al-Ashari responds by affirming that the reward is distinct from Allah and that shifting from literal to metaphorical interpretation requires evidence. He then strengthens his case with a compelling analogy: if we allow arbitrary metaphorical leaps, even clear commands like 'pray to Me' could be twisted.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the objector's claim and al-Ashari's immediate response.
Notice the objector suggests a metaphorical meaning for 'looking at their Lord'.
2
Understand the principle of literal vs. metaphorical
Al-Ashari states that departing from literal meaning (ḥaqīqa) to metaphorical (majāz) requires proof (ḥujja) or evidence (dalāla). Reflect on why this principle is important for interpreting revelation.
This principle safeguards scripture from arbitrary interpretation.
3
Analyze the analogy
Al-Ashari gives an example: if Allah said 'pray to Me and worship Me', no one could interpret 'Me' as someone else. Understand how this analogy supports his argument.
The analogy shows the absurd consequences of allowing metaphorical interpretation without evidence.
4
Apply the principle to the verse on vision
Connect the analogy back to the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' (6:103). Realize that if the same metaphorical liberty were applied, the meaning would become incoherent. Conclude that the literal reading is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : ما أنكرتُمْ أن يكون قوله -تعالى-: ﴿إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ أي : إِلى ثَوابِ ربِّها ناظِرة؟ قيل له : ثوابُ اللَّهِ -تعالى- غيرُه ، ولا يجوز أن يُعْدَلَ بالكلام عن الحقيقة إلى المجازِ بغيرِ حُجَّةٍ ولا دِلَالَةٍ . أَلا ترى أنَّ اللَّهَ -تعالى- لو قال: «صلُّوا إلى واعبدوني» لم يجز أن يقول قائل : عَنى غيره، ولو جاز هذا لجاز لزاعم أن يزعم أن قوله: لا تُدْرِكُهُ أراد به أنها لا تُدرِك غيرَه الأبصارُ .
If a questioner says: 'Why do you deny that His statement, Exalted is He: {Looking at their Lord} means: looking at the reward of their Lord?' It is said to him: 'The reward of Allah, Exalted is He, is other than Him, and it is not permissible to turn speech from literal meaning to metaphorical without proof or evidence. Do you not see that if Allah, Exalted is He, said: 'Pray to Me and worship Me,' it would not be permissible for someone to say: 'He meant someone else.' And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible for a claimant to claim that His statement: 'No vision can grasp Him' meant that they do not grasp other than Him with their eyes.'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector suggests that 'looking at their Lord' in the Quran actually means looking at His reward, not at His essence. al-Ashari replies by affirming that the reward is distinct from Allah and that it is not permissible to depart from the literal meaning to a metaphorical one without proof. He supports this with a logical analogy: if Allah commanded 'pray to Me and worship Me', no one could interpret it as referring to someone else; similarly, if one could arbitrarily reinterpret 'no vision can grasp Him' to mean 'they do not grasp other than Him,' that would be absurd. Thus, the literal reading must be maintained.

Key Terms
الحقيقة
المجاز
التأويل
الحجة
الدلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'looking at their Lord' in the Quran means looking at His reward, not at His essence.
Correction: Al-Ashari argues that this is an unjustified metaphorical interpretation; the literal meaning is looking at Allah Himself, and no proof has been given to depart from it.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ashari reject interpreting 'looking at their Lord' as looking at His reward?
A) Because the reward is the same as Allah.
B) Because there is no proof or evidence to depart from the literal meaning.
C) Because the verse is metaphorical by consensus.
D) Because the reward does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Literal meaning of Quranic verses about seeing Allah must be upheld unless there is clear evidence for metaphorical interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'Looking at their Lord,' does your heart long for the actual vision of Allah, or do you instinctively rationalize it away to avoid the weight of that promise? What would change in your worship if you truly believed you will see Him with your own eyes?
?
Are there verses of the Quran that you have subtly reinterpreted to fit your own comfort or worldview, rather than accepting their literal meaning? How can you train yourself to submit to Allah's words even when they challenge your reasoning?
?

Why Temporal Limitation Does Not Apply to the Slumber Verse
al-Ashari distinguishes between verses where temporal limitation applies and those where it does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'Neither slumber nor sleep can seize Him' cannot be temporally limited, based on al-Ashari's reasoning about the presence or absence of conflicting Quranic texts.

Core Idea
One verse's temporal limitation does not justify another's; context matters.

In this passage, al-Ashari addresses an objection that tries to generalize temporal limitation from the vision verse to the slumber verse. He explains a crucial principle: temporal harmonization is only needed when there are two apparently conflicting verses. Since the slumber verse has no counterpart, it remains absolute. This teaches us a key method of Quranic interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the objector's claim that if the vision verse can be temporally limited, the slumber verse should be too. Notice the parallel structure.
2
Identify al-Ashari's Answer
Read al-Ashari's reply carefully. He points out a key difference between the two cases.
3
Understand the Difference
Al-Ashari explains that the vision verse has another verse about 'faces looking at Him,' so both must be harmonized. The slumber verse has no such contradicting verse.
This is the core of the argument: the presence of a second verse demands temporal limitation; its absence leaves the verse absolute.
4
Apply the Principle
Reflect on the general principle: temporal limitation is a method of reconciling conflicting texts, not a default interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : فإذا كان قولُهُ: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ في وقتٍ دونَ وقتٍ، فما أنكرتَ أنْ يكونَ قوله: ﴿وَلَا تَأْخُذُهُ سِنَةٌ وَلَا نَوْمٌ﴾ في وقتٍ دُونَ وقت؟ قيل لَهُ : الفرقُ بينَهُما أَنَّهُ قالَ لَنَا في آية : إنه لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ، وقال فى آيةٍ أُخْرَى : إنَّ الوُجوهَ تَنْظُرُ إليهِ (فاستعملنا الآيتين، وقلنا : إن المعنى في ذلك : أنَّها تنظُرُ إليه) في وقتٍ وَلا تُدرِكُهُ في وقت.
If he says: 'If His statement: No vision can grasp Him is in one time not another, then why do you deny that His statement: {Neither slumber nor sleep can seize Him} is in one time not another?' It is said to him: 'The difference between them is that He said to us in one verse: No vision can grasp Him, and He said in another verse: Faces will look at Him (so we applied both verses and said that the meaning is that they look at Him) at one time and do not grasp Him at another time.'
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage contains a third objection and answer. The objector tries to apply the same temporal limitation to the verse about Allah not being seized by slumber or sleep. al-Ashari answers by pointing out a key difference: for the vision verses, there are two Quranic texts—one denying vision and one affirming it—so harmonization requires temporal distinction. But for the slumber verse, there is no other verse to conflict with it, so it remains absolute. Thus, the analogy fails.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one verse can be temporally limited, all verses can be temporally limited.
Correction: Temporal limitation is only applied when there are two apparently conflicting verses that need harmonization. If no conflicting verse exists, the verse remains general and absolute, as with the slumber verse.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the verse on vision and the verse on slumber, according to al-Ashari?
A) The slumber verse has no contradictory verse, while vision has two verses.
B) The slumber verse is metaphorical, while vision is literal.
C) The slumber verse applies only to the Hereafter.
D) The slumber verse was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Temporal limitation is a tool for reconciling conflicting texts; without a conflicting verse, the verse remains absolute in its meaning.

?
When you encounter two verses that seem to say different things, do you feel tempted to ignore one or twist both? Al-Ashari shows that reconciling verses requires careful study. What step will you take this week to better understand a pair of verses that confuse you?

Refuting the Analogy Between Sleep and Vision of God
Ash'ari refutes an analogy between sleep and vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari refutes the analogy between sleep and vision of God by distinguishing the nature of sleep as an affliction affecting the subject, whereas vision does not affect the object.

Core Idea
Sleep is an affliction; vision is not — the analogy breaks.

This passage from Imam al-Ash'ari's al-Luma' presents a key argument in the debate on the vision of God (ru'ya). The opponent attempts to use the Quranic mention of sleep to argue that if God could be seen, He could also be subject to sleep. Ash'ari refutes this by pointing out the fundamental difference between sleep and vision. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ash'ari structures his refutation in two parts.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
The opponent uses verses about sleep to argue that if God could be seen, He could also sleep. Find this in the passage.
3
Understand Ash'ari's two refutation points
First, Ash'ari notes that God did not consistently attribute sleep to Himself. Second, he distinguishes sleep as an affliction on the sleeper from vision as not an affliction on the seen.
The key concept is 'affliction' (آفة).
4
See how the analogy is invalidated
Since sleep is an imperfection (removing knowledge), it cannot apply to God, but vision is not an imperfection, so the analogy fails.
This is a direct refutation of the opponent's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في الرؤية ولم يقل لنا في آية: إن السنة والنوم يأخذانه، وفي آية أخرى لا يأخذانه، فيستعمل ذلك في وقتين. وأيضًا فإن النوم آفة تقوم بالنائم تزيل عنه العلم، وليست الرؤية آفة تحل في المرئي فيجب منع الرؤية بمثل ما به وجب منع النوم.
Chapter on the Discussion of Vision. And He did not say to us in a verse: 'Truly drowsiness and sleep take Him,' and in another verse they do not take Him, so that (sleep) is used at two times. And also, sleep is an affliction that occurs in the sleeper, removing him from knowledge, and vision is not an affliction that occurs in the seen thing, so it is necessary to prevent vision by the same (reason) by which sleep is prevented.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the possibility of seeing God. The opponent argues that if God could be seen, then He could also be subject to sleep, citing Quranic verses that mention sleep. Ash'ari refutes this analogy by distinguishing between sleep and vision. He states that sleep is an affliction (آفة) that removes knowledge from the sleeper, whereas vision is not an affliction that affects the seen object. Therefore, the reasoning used to deny sleep from God (because it is an imperfection) does not apply to vision, since vision is not an imperfection. Thus, the analogy is invalid.

Key Terms

آفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ash'ari is arguing that vision is not an affliction, so it is permissible for God to be seen. But the key point is that sleep is an affliction, breaking the analogy.
Correction: Ash'ari's argument is not about the permissibility of vision per se, but about the invalidity of the analogy: because sleep is an affliction and vision is not, the reasoning that denies sleep to God does not extend to vision.

Checkpoint

A) Sleep is an affliction that removes knowledge, but vision is not.
B) Sleep is a natural phenomenon.
C) Vision is similar to sleep.
D) The Quran prohibits sleep.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari refutes the analogy by showing that sleep is an affliction on the subject, while vision is not an affliction on the object, so the reasoning for denying sleep does not apply to vision.

Refutation of the Extension from Seeing to Other Senses
Ash'ari rebuts the claim that visible God implies sensible God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the claim that if God is visible then He must also be sensible through other senses.

Core Idea
Ash'ari turns the opponent's logic against itself, showing it proves too much.

In this passage, Ash'ari responds to an objection against the possibility of seeing God. The opponent argues that if God could be seen despite being unlike physical objects, then by the same reasoning He could be touched, tasted, and smelled. Ash'ari counters with a clever analogy that exposes the flaw in the opponent's logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts
Read the passage and identify the opponent's statement and Ash'ari's reply. Notice how the opponent's argument is structured.
2
Analyze the opponent's reasoning
The opponent claims that if God can be seen despite being unlike seen objects, then He can also be touched, tasted, and smelled despite being unlike those objects. This is an argument by analogy.
3
Examine Ash'ari's counter-analogy
Ash'ari points out that the opponent already accepts God has attributes like seeing without being similar. By the same logic, one could argue God has touching, tasting, and smelling, which the opponent rejects. This shows the original reasoning is invalid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why the opponent's argument fails: attributes are not transferable by analogy in the way they assumed. The attributes of God are unique and not subject to the same rules as creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is said to them: What is the difference between you and someone who says: If it is permissible for the Eternal to be seeing, knowing, powerful, living, not like seers, knowers, powerful, living, then it would be permissible for Him to be touching, tasting, smelling, not like touchers, tasters, smellers? If this is not necessary, then why do you deny that what you said is not necessary?
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer concerning the visibility of God. The opponent argues that if God can be seen despite being unlike physical objects (مرئيات), then by the same logic He could also be touched, tasted, and smelled. Ash'ari replies with a counter-analogy: the opponent already accepts that God is seeing, knowing, powerful, and living, even though He is unlike creatures in these respects. Similarly, one could argue that if these attributes are accepted, then God could also be a toucher, taster, and smeller. But since the opponent does not accept that, the original objection is shown to be invalid. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum, demonstrating that the opponent's reasoning is not necessary.

Correction: Ash'ari is using a reductio ad absurdum to show the opponent's logic is flawed; he does not affirm these attributes.

B) By showing that the same logic would allow God to have other attributes like seeing and knowing without requiring tasting.
C) By citing Quranic verses.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari refutes the opponent by showing that their reasoning would lead to unacceptable conclusions, proving that the original objection is not valid.

Refuting the Objection from Other Senses to Ru'ya
Objection and answer regarding ru'ya and other senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari refutes the objection that if God can be seen unlike visible things, then He could also be touched, tasted, and smelled.

Core Idea
The objection's own logic is turned against it through a parallel argument about attributes.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari handles a challenge from those who argue that allowing vision of God without likeness opens the door to other sensory attributes. He responds by drawing an analogy from the way God possesses attributes like knowledge without being like knowers. The objection fails because it proves too much.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objector sets up the analogy from sight to other senses.
The objector uses a qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (reasoning from the unseen to the seen) type of argument.
2
Understand al-Ash'ari's parallel argument
Al-Ash'ari counters by drawing a parallel: if God can have attributes like knowledge without being like knowers, then by the same logic He could have sensory qualities. Identify the two parallels and see how they mirror each other.
This is a classical refutation by turning the objection against the objector.
3
Grasp the logical conclusion
Al-Ash'ari ends by pointing out that if the objector rejects his parallel, then they cannot insist on their own parallel either. Understand why this forces the objector to abandon their objection.
The argument exposes the inconsistency in the objector's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو جاز أن يرى القديم سبحانه وليس كالمرئيات، لجاز أن يلمس ويذاق ويشم وليس كالمذوقات ولا كالملموسات ولا كالمشمومات. قيل لهم: ما الفرق بينكم وبين من قال: لو جاز أن يكون القديم رائيا عالما قادرا حيا لا كالرائين العلماء القادرين الأحياء، لجاز أن يكون لامسا ذائقا شاما لا كاللامسين الذائقين الشامين. فإن لم يجب هذا فما أنكرتم من أن لا يجب ما قلتموه؟
If they say: If it is permissible that the Eternal (glory be to Him) be seen, and He is not like visible things, then it would be permissible that He be touched, tasted, and smelled, and He is not like tasted things, nor like touched things, nor like smelled things. It is said to them: What is the difference between you and the one who says: If it is permissible that the Eternal be seeing, knowing, able, living, not like the seeing, knowing, able, living, then it would be permissible that He be touching, tasting, smelling, not like the touching, tasting, smelling. If this is not incumbent, then why do you deny that what you say is not incumbent?
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the vision of God (ru'ya). The objectors argue that if God can be seen without being like visible objects, then by the same logic He could be touched, tasted, and smelled without being like those objects. The author (al-Ash'ari) refutes this by turning the analogy back on them: if God can be seeing, knowing, etc. without being like other beings that have these attributes, then by the same logic He could be touching, tasting, etc. without being like them. If the objectors do not accept this parallel, then they cannot insist on their own parallel either. This demonstrates that the analogy from visible things to other senses is arbitrary and not binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think al-Ash'ari is claiming God can be touched or tasted.
Correction: He is only showing the logical inconsistency of the objection, not asserting that God has these qualities.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Ash'ari's response to the objection about other senses?
A) He concedes that God could be touched, tasted, and smelled.
B) He argues that if the objectors reject the parallel from attributes, they cannot use a parallel from senses.
C) He says the original argument only applies to sight, not other senses.
D) He denies that God can be seen.

Key Takeaway
The objection from other senses is refuted by showing it proves too much, using a parallel argument that the objector cannot reject without also rejecting their own premise.

Allah Creates the True Nature of Disbelief and Faith
Al-Ash'ari proves that Allah alone creates the reality of disbelief and faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's proof that Allah is the sole creator of the essential reality of disbelief and faith, not the human agent.

Core Idea
The real nature of an act is created by Allah; humans intend but do not originate reality.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari presents a sharp logical argument rooted in the nature of intention. He shows that the disbeliever and the believer cannot be the true originators of their own disbelief and faith because their intentions conflict with the actual reality of those acts. This forces the conclusion that only Allah, who intends the true nature of each act, can be the creator of disbelief and faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the argument's flow settle before analyzing it.
The passage is dense; reading it aloud can help.
2
Identify the general principle
Notice the opening conditional: if an action could occur in reality without an intender, then all actions might lack intention. This sets up the necessity of an intending agent for every real action.
This is a qiyas-style argument.
3
Apply to disbelief and faith
See how the principle applies to disbelief: the disbeliever wants it good, but it is evil, so he cannot be its originator. Similarly for faith: the believer wants it easy, but it is difficult.
Focus on the conflict between intention and reality.
4
Follow to the conclusion
Since neither the disbeliever nor the believer can be the originator, the only possibility is Allah, who intends the true nature. The argument about bodies (bodies cannot act on other bodies) reinforces this.
This conclusion affirms divine omnipotence.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument supports the Ash'ari position that Allah creates all human actions, while humans acquire them (kasb). It does not deny human choice, but rather assigns creation to Allah.
This connects to the qadar debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على ما هو عليه، وهو قاصد إلى ذلك؛ لأنَّه لو جاز حدوثُ فِعل، على الحقيقة، لا مِنْ قاصدِ، لَمْ يُوْمَنْ أن تكونَ الأفعالُ كلُّهَا كذلك، كما أنَّه لو جازَ حدوث فعل لا مِنْ فاعِل لم يُؤْمَنْ أن تكون الأفعالُ كلُّها كذلِكَ . وإذا كان هذا هكذا ، فقد وجب أن يكونَ لِلْكُفْرِ مُحدِثٌ أحدثَهُ كُفرًا باطلا قبيحًا، وهو قاصد إلى ذلك، ولَنْ يجوز أن يكونَ المُحدِثُ لَهُ هُوَ الكافِرَ ، الذي يُرِيدُ أن يكونَ الكُفْرُ حَسَنًا صَوابًا حقًّا ، فيكون على خِلافِ ذلك . وكذلك للإيمان محدِثٌ أحدَثَه، على حقيقته متعباً مؤلماً مُرْمِضًا ، غيرُ المؤمن الذي لو جَهِدَ أن يقع الإيمانُ خلاف ما وقع، مِنْ إيلامِهِ وإتعابه وإرماضه، لم يكنْ لَهُ إلى ذلِكَ سَبِيلٌ . وإذا لم يجز أن يكونَ المحدِثُ للكفر - على حقيقتِهِ - الكافر، وَلَا المحدِثُ للإيمانِ على حقيقته المؤمِنَ، فَقَدْ وَجَبَ أن يكونَ مُحدِثُ ذلك هُوَ اللَّهَ - تعالى - ربَّ العالمينَ، القاصِدَ إلى ذلِكَ ؛ لأنه لا يجوز أن يكونَ أحدَثَ ذلك جسمٌ مِنَ الأجْسامِ؛ لأنَّ الأجسام لا يجوزُ أن تفعل في غيرها شيئًا .
Upon what it is, and He is intending that; because if an action could occur, in reality, not from an intender, it would not be safe that all actions are likewise, just as if an action could occur without a doer, it would not be safe that all actions are likewise. And if this is so, then it is necessary that for disbelief there is an originator who originated it as false, evil, ugly disbelief, and He is intending that, and it is not permissible that the originator of it be the disbeliever, who desires that disbelief be good, correct, true, so it is contrary to that. And similarly for faith there is an originator who originated it, in its reality, as tiring, painful, distressing, other than the believer who if he exerted himself that faith occur contrary to what occurred, from its pain, tiredness, and distress, he would have no way to that. And when it is not permissible that the originator of disbelief - in its reality - be the disbeliever, nor the originator of faith in its reality be the believer, then it is necessary that the originator of that be Allah - exalted - Lord of the worlds, intending that; because it is not permissible that a body among bodies originated that; because bodies cannot act in other than themselves at all.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a proof that Allah is the sole creator of disbelief and faith. Al-Ash'ari begins with a general principle: if an action could occur in reality without an intended purpose, then all actions might be without purpose, which is absurd. Similarly, if an action could occur without a doer, all actions might be doerless. Since this is impossible, every action must have an intender and a doer. Applying this to disbelief: disbelief is in reality false and evil, but the disbeliever wishes it to be good and true. Therefore, the disbeliever cannot be its originator, because he wants the opposite of its actual nature. Likewise, faith is in reality tiring and painful, but the believer would prefer it to be easy and pleasant. So the believer cannot be its originator either. Thus, the only possible originator is Allah, who intends the true nature of each act. Al-Ash'ari then adds a supporting argument: bodies (i.e., physical beings) cannot act upon other bodies; only Allah can create these realities. This establishes the Ash'ari position that Allah creates all human actions, including belief and disbelief.

Key Terms
مُحدِث (originator)
كفر (disbelief)
إيمان (faith)
قاصد (intender)
حقيقة (reality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage denies human free will entirely.
Correction: The passage only argues that the essential reality of an act (its goodness/evil, pain/comfort) is created by Allah; it does not negate human intention or acquisition (kasb). Humans freely choose and acquire the act, but its ultimate nature is from Allah.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Ash'ari argue that the disbeliever cannot be the originator of his own disbelief?
A) Because the disbeliever has no power over his actions.
B) Because the disbeliever intends disbelief to be good, but it is actually evil.
C) Because the disbeliever does not exist.
D) Because disbelief is created by another human.

Key Takeaway
Allah creates the essential reality of every act, including disbelief and faith, as evidenced by the mismatch between human intention and actual nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle in your faith—finding it tiring, painful, or distressing—do you recognize that Allah created that very reality for a purpose? How does this change your response to hardship in worship, rather than resenting it?
?
If disbelief is fundamentally false and ugly in Allah's sight, yet the disbeliever genuinely thinks it is good and true, consider your own heart: what sin or attitude do you rationalize as good that you need to repent from, acknowledging that its reality is something else?
?
The believer would prefer faith to be easy, but Allah made it such that it requires effort and pain. Are you willing to accept the cost of faith—its demands on your time, comfort, and desires—or do you secretly wish for a religion that costs you nothing? What one thing will you change today to embrace the true nature of faith?
