Proof of God's Power to Grant Grace for Faith
Ashari proves God's ability to grant grace for faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari proves that God is capable of granting a grace that would lead disbelievers to believe, using Quranic verses and the nature of capacity.

Core Idea
If God withholds provision to prevent transgression, He can grant grace to cause faith.

This passage from al-Luma' addresses a key question in Ash'ari theology: Is God able to lead disbelievers to faith through a divine grace? Imam Ashari answers with a clear proof based on two Quranic verses and a logical argument about the nature of capacity. As we read, notice how he uses the principle of analogy: if God can cause disbelief by withholding something, He can also cause faith by granting something.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind.
The passage begins with a question from an opponent.
2
Identify the question and immediate answer
The opponent asks: Is God capable of a grace that would cause disbelievers to believe? The answer is 'Yes'. Notice that the proof follows immediately.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Two verses are cited. The first (42:27) shows God withholds provision to prevent transgression. The second (43:33) shows God withholds luxury to prevent universal disbelief. Both illustrate divine restraint.
These verses establish that God's actions are based on wisdom, not inability.
4
Understand the second proof on capacity
The second argument links capacity (istitāʿa) to the act itself. If God can enable faith, He can bring about the act of faith that results from that enablement.
This reinforces the conclusion from a different angle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في التعديل والتجوير
نفي وجوب الصلاح والأصلح
فإن قال قائل : هل يقدِرُ اللَّهُ على لُطف لو فعله بالكافرين لآمنوا ؟ قيل لهم : نعم ؛ والدليل على ذلك أنَّه يقدِرُ أن يفعل بالمؤمنين وبعباده ، ما لو فعله بهم لَبَغَوْا في الأرض، قال الله -تعالى-: وَلَوْ بَسَطَ اللهُ الرِّزْقَ لِعِبَادِهِ لَبَغَوْا فِي الْأَرْضِ، وقال: وَلَوْلَا أَن يَكُونَ النَّاسُ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً يعني على الكفر لَجَعَلْنَا لِمَن يَكْفُرُ بِالرَّحْمَنِ لِبُيُوتِهِمْ سُقْفًا مِّن فِضَّةٍ وَمَعَارِجَ عَلَيْهَا يَظْهَرُونَ. فلمَّا كانَ الله تعالى قادِرًا على أن يفعلَ بالخَلْقِ مَا لَوْ فعلَه بهِمْ كَفَرُوا ، كان قادِرًا أن يفعلَ بِهم ما لَوْ فعله لآمَنُوا . وأيضًا : فقد دللنا على أن في كون الاستطاعةِ كَوْنَ الفِعْلِ، فإذا كانَ قادرًا على إقدارِهم على الإيمان، فهُوَ قادِرٌ على أن يفعل ما فَعَله بهم لآمَنُوا.
Chapter on justice and injustice. Denial of the obligation of what is right and best. If someone says: Is God capable of a grace such that if He did it to the disbelievers they would believe? It is said to them: Yes. The evidence for that is that He is capable of doing to the believers and His servants that which if He did to them they would transgress on earth. God Almighty said: 'And if God had extended provision for His servants, they would have transgressed on earth' (42:27). And He said: 'And if it were not that the people would become one community' (43:33) – meaning on disbelief – 'We would have made for those who disbelieve in the Most Merciful, for their houses, ceilings of silver and stairways upon which they ascend' (43:33). Since God Almighty is capable of doing to creation that which if He did they would disbelieve, He is capable of doing to them that which if He did they would believe. Also: we have demonstrated that the existence of capacity is the existence of the act, and if He is capable of enabling them to have faith, then He is capable of doing that which if He did to them they would believe.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God is capable of granting disbelievers a grace (luṭf) that would lead them to faith. The author begins with a question: Is God capable of such a grace? The answer is yes. The evidence is drawn from two Quranic verses. The first verse (42:27) shows that if God had expanded provision for His servants, they would have transgressed – meaning God withholds provision to prevent transgression. Similarly, if God were to grant a grace that would cause faith, but He withholds it, that does not imply inability. The second verse (43:33) indicates that if it were not for the risk of all people becoming one community in disbelief, God would have given the disbelievers luxurious houses. This shows God can grant good things to disbelievers but refrains for a greater wisdom. The conclusion is: since God can cause disbelief by withholding grace, He can also cause faith by granting grace. A second proof is presented: the nature of capacity (istitāʿa) is such that it is identical with the act itself. God has power over enabling people to have faith, so He has power over the act of faith that would result from that enablement.

Key Terms
لطف (luṭf)
استطاعة (istitāʿa)
إيمان (īmān)
كفر (kufr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God can grant faith to disbelievers, He must do so, making them blameworthy for not believing.
Correction: The proof only establishes God's ability, not obligation. God withholds grace for wise reasons, and humans remain responsible.

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence Ashari uses to show that God can grant a grace that would cause disbelievers to believe?
A) Logical deduction from the nature of capacity
B) Quranic verses showing God prevents transgression by withholding provision
C) Consensus of the scholars
D) Direct revelation from the Prophet

Key Takeaway
God is fully capable of granting a grace that leads to faith, but that ability does not necessitate its bestowal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is fully capable of granting you faith but may choose not to for a wisdom you do not see, what does that demand of you today? Are you assuming His grace is automatic, or are you urgently responding to the means of faith He has already given you?
?
The verses show that God withholds worldly goods to prevent transgression. Think of a limitation in your life—health, wealth, or comfort. Could it be that this limitation is actually a mercy preventing you from greater sin? How does that change your attitude toward your current circumstances?
?
You now know that God's ability to give faith does not obligate Him to do so. How does that truth confront your own negligence? Are you actively seeking faith, or passively waiting for a divine intervention you have no guarantee of receiving?

Defining Stinginess: Obligation vs. Favor
Ashari defines stinginess and explains why withholding optional grace is not stingy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why withholding optional grace is not considered stinginess according to Ashari's definition of stinginess as failing to do what is obligatory.

Core Idea
Stinginess is failing to do what is obligatory, not withholding optional favors.

This passage addresses a common objection against God's justice: if God could make disbelievers believe by granting them grace, why doesn't He? The objector claims this withholding is stingy. Ashari responds by defining stinginess precisely and showing it does not apply to optional acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a hypothetical objection followed by a response.
The objector uses the word 'bakhila' (stingy) as an accusation.
2
Identify the definition of stinginess
Find where Ashari defines stinginess (al-bukhl). Write down the definition: 'not doing what one is obligated to do.'
3
Distinguish between obligation and favor
Notice the contrast Ashari makes between what is obligatory (wājib) and what is a favor (tafaḍḍul). Understand that favors are not required.
The key term 'tafaḍḍul' appears twice.
4
Apply to the objection
Since God's grace is a favor, not an obligation, withholding it does not constitute stinginess. Reflect on why this refutes the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال : فإذا لم يَفْعَلْ بالكفار ما يُؤْمِنُونَ عنده فقد بَخِلَ عليهم؟ قيل له : البخل : أن لا يفعل الفاعل ما يجب عليه فعله، فأما ما كان تفضلا : فللمتفضل أن يتفضل به، وله أن لا يتفضل به، وما كان تفضلا لم يلحق البخل - في أن لا يفعله - الفاعل.
If he says: If He does not do to the disbelievers that by which they would believe, then He has been stingy with them? It is said to him: Stinginess is that the agent does not do what is obligatory for him to do. As for that which is a favor: the one who bestows favors may bestow it, and he may not bestow it, and what is a favor does not attach stinginess to the agent in not doing it.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector claims that if God withholds a grace that would cause faith, then God is stingy (bakhīl). Ashari answers by defining stinginess: it consists in failing to do what one is obligated to do. Acts of favor (tafaḍḍul) are not obligatory; the one who grants favors may choose to grant them or not. Since God's withholding of grace is not a failure to fulfill an obligation, it cannot be considered stingy. The key point is that stinginess is only relevant in the context of obligations, not optional generosity.

Key Terms
بخل (bukh)
وجوب (wujūb)
تفضل (tafaḍḍul)
منع (manʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think any withholding of good is stinginess.
Correction: Ashari clarifies that stinginess only applies when one fails to do what is obligatory, not when one withholds optional favors.

Checkpoint

A) Not doing a favor that one could do
B) Not doing what one is obligated to do
C) Withholding any good from others
D) Doing something harmful

Key Takeaway
Stinginess is defined by failing to fulfill an obligation, not by withholding optional favors; thus God's withholding of grace is not stingy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

God's Will Includes Disbelief of Disbelievers
Ashari answers an objection about God's will for disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ashari affirms God's will extends to the disbelief of disbelievers, and that this is consistent with divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
God wills all that occurs, including disbelief, without injustice.

In this exchange, an objector presses a logical consequence of Ashari's doctrine: if God withholds the grace needed for belief, does He actually will disbelief? Ashari's blunt 'yes' and his reference to earlier arguments show that divine will is all-encompassing. This lesson examines that response and its theological grounding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a direct answer.
The Arabic is short but carries significant weight.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The objector asks: if God does not do what would cause belief, does He will their foolishness and disbelief? Ashari answers 'Yes' and refers to earlier discussion. Identify these two parts.
The answer is immediate and unambiguous.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: God's will is not limited to good; it encompasses all actual events, including disbelief. This is a core Ashari position on divine attributes.
This contrasts with views that limit God's will to good only.

Source Text

Ashari answers plainly: 'Yes'. He then refers to earlier discussion where the universal scope of God's will was established. The point is that God's will is not limited to good; it includes everything that occurs, including disbelief. This is consistent with Ashari's doctrine of comprehensive divine will.

Ashari affirms that God wills the folly and disbelief of disbelievers.

Refuting That God’s Will for Disbelief Makes Him Foolish
Ashari refutes the claim that God would be foolish for intending disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari refutes the objection that God would be foolish if He intends disbelief, using an analogy from creation.

Core Idea
God's intention of disbelief does not imply foolishness, just as creating a disbeliever does not.

Imam Ashari engages with a theological objection: if God intends disbelief, does that make Him foolish? In this passage, he presents a crisp argument showing that the objection is inconsistent. By comparing the case of creation (where God creates someone knowing they will disbelieve) with the case of intention, he demonstrates that both are acts of wisdom, not folly. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the opponent's claim: 'The one who intends folly is foolish.'
This is the central objection Ashari refutes.
2
Understand Ashari's analogical reasoning
Ashari draws an analogy: if creating a disbeliever does not make God foolish, then intending their disbelief also does not. Trace the logical steps in the passage.
3
Reflect on why this preserves divine transcendence
Consider how this argument upholds God's perfection—His actions and intentions are never foolish, as they are grounded in wisdom.
This connects to the broader theme of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في التعديل والتجوير. -٣٨- مسألة. ثُمَّ يُقالُ لهم : إنْ كانَ اللَّهُ -تعالى- إذا لم يَفْعَلْ بهِم مَا يُؤْمِنونَ عندَه يَجِبُ أَنْ يُرِيدَ فَسادَهم، فما أنكرتُم من أنَّه إذَا خَلَقَهُم - وهو يعلمُ أنَّهم يكفرون - فقد أرادَ كُفْرَهم؟ فإنْ قالُوا : مُريدُ السَّفْهِ سَفِيهٌ . قيل لهم : أليس خالقُ مَنْ يَعلَمُ أنَّه يَكْفُرُ، لا يكون سفيها بخَلْقِهِ، ولا يكونُ خَلْقُهُ إِيَّاه سَفَهَا ، فَمَا أَنكَرْتُمْ أنْ يكونَ الخالقُ إذا أرادَ سفَهَهم لم يكن سفيها ؟ وقد تكلمنا في هذه المسألة قبل هذا الموضع .
Chapter: On the subject of justice and injustice. - 38 - Question: Then it is said to them: If God, exalted, when He does not do to them that at which they believe, it is necessary that He intends their corruption, then why do you not deny that when He created them – and He knows that they will disbelieve – then He has intended their disbelief? If they say: The one who intends folly is foolish. It is said to them: Is not the creator of one whom He knows will disbelieve not foolish for creating him, and His creation of him is not folly? So why do you deny that the creator, when He intends their folly, is not foolish? And we have spoken on this matter before this place.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a new section and a self-contained argument. Ashari begins with a chapter heading on 'al-ta'dil wa al-tajwir' (justice and injustice). Then he presents a question (mas'ala). He argues: if the opponent claims that when God does not cause belief, He must intend their corruption (fasād), then why do they not also accept that when God creates them knowing they will disbelieve, He intended their disbelief? The opponent objects: 'One who intends folly is foolish' (murīdu al-safahi safīhun). Ashari replies by analogy: The Creator who creates someone knowing he will disbelieve is not considered foolish for creating him, nor is His creation folly. So similarly, if the Creator intends their folly, He is not foolish. The argument is that God's knowledge and power do not entail foolishness in His actions; rather, He acts with wisdom. Ashari concludes by noting he has discussed this elsewhere.

Key Terms
التعديل والتجوير (al-ta'dil wa al-tajwir)
السفه (al-safah)
المريد (al-murīd)
الخلق (al-khalq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What objection do the opponents raise against God intending folly?
A) God is too exalted to intend anything.
B) The one who intends folly is foolish.
C) God only intends good.
D) There is no proof God intends anything.

Key Takeaway
God’s intention of disbelief does not make Him foolish, because His actions are always wise and just.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone persist in disbelief, do you ever quietly question God's wisdom? How does Ashari's argument force you to trust that God's will is never foolish, even when you don't understand it?
?
Is there a situation in your life where you've resented God's will because it seemed to lead to something 'bad'? After reading this passage, will you let go of that resentment and submit to His perfect wisdom?
?
If you truly believe that God's intention of disbelief is not foolish, how should that change the way you speak about or to those who disbelieve? Will you stop judging God's plan and instead pray for guidance?

God’s Will and the Charge of Foolishness
Ash'ari refutes the charge that willing disbelief makes God foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari defends God's will against the charge of foolishness by using a logical analogy.

Core Idea
God's will is not judged by human standards of foolishness.

This passage addresses a key objection against divine predestination: if God wills disbelief, does that make Him foolish? Ash'ari responds with a powerful argument by analogy, showing that creating a disbeliever with foreknowledge is not foolish, so willing disbelief is likewise not foolish. The argument upholds God's absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
The passage is from the chapter on declaring God just and unjust.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
Notice that the opponents claim that if God wills foolishness (disbelief), then He must be foolish. Underline this claim in the text.
The objection is based on a human moral analogy.
3
Understand Ash'ari's analogical argument
Ash'ari asks: Is the Creator of someone He knows will disbelieve considered foolish? If not, then willing their disbelief is also not foolish. Follow the logic step by step.
The argument uses the same reasoning the opponents applied against God.
4
Reflect on the implication for divine attributes
Consider what this argument says about God's justice and wisdom. Why can't we apply human standards of foolishness to God?
This connects to broader themes in Kalam about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في التعديل والتجويرِ -٣٨- مسألة ثُمَّ يُقالُ لهم : إنْ كانَ اللَّهُ -تعالى- إذا لم يَفْعَلْ بهِم مَا يُؤْمِنونَ عندَه يَجِبُ أَنْ يُرِيدَ فَسادَهم، فما أنكرتُم من أنَّه إذَا خَلَقَهُم - وهو يعلمُ أنَّهم يكفرون - فقد أرادَ كُفْرَهم؟ فإنْ قالُوا : مُريدُ السَّفْهِ سَفِيةٌ . قيل لهم : أليس خالقُ مَنْ يَعلَمُ أنَّه يَكْفُرُ، لا يكون سفيها بخَلْقِهِ، ولا يكونُ خَلْقُهُ إِيَّاه سَفَهَا ، فَمَا أَنكَرْتُمْ أنْ يكونَ الخالقُ إذا أرادَ سفَهَهم لم يكن سفيها ؟ وقد تكلمنا في هذه المسألة قبل هذا الموضع
Chapter on the discourse concerning declaring [God] just and unjust. - Issue 38 - Then it is said to them: If God - the Exalted - when He does not do for them what they would believe through it, must intend their corruption, then what prevents you from [acknowledging] that when He created them - knowing that they would disbelieve - He thereby willed their disbelief? If they say: The one who wills foolishness is foolish. It is said to them: Is not the Creator of one whom He knows will disbelieve not foolish by His creating him, and His creating him is not foolishness? So what prevents you from [acknowledging] that the Creator, if He willed their foolishness, would not be foolish? And we have discussed this issue before this place.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by opponents regarding God's will and human actions. The author (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) presents an argument against those who claim that if God does not enable people to believe, then He must intend their corruption (فساد). The argument proceeds as follows:

1. The opponents are confronted with a logical consequence: If God creates a person while knowing that the person will disbelieve, then according to their own reasoning, God must have willed that person's disbelief.
2. The opponents object: 'The one who wills foolishness (سفه) is foolish.' They imply that if God wills disbelief, He would be foolish.
3. The author responds by drawing an analogy: The Creator of a person whom He knows will disbelieve is not considered foolish by the mere act of creating him, and the creation itself is not foolish. So why would the Creator be foolish if He willed their foolishness? The same logic applies.
4. The author notes that this issue has been discussed earlier in the book.

Thus, the passage defends God's absolute sovereignty and rejects anthropomorphic attributions of foolishness to God. The key point is that God's knowledge and creation of disbelief do not necessitate that He is foolish or unjust; rather, His actions are beyond human criterion of foolishness.

Key Terms

إرادة
سفه
فساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that God's knowledge of disbelief means He approves or wills it in a morally blameworthy sense.
Correction: Ash'ari argues that God's will is not subject to human moral categories; creating a disbeliever is not foolish, so willing disbelief is not foolish either.

Checkpoint

B) He argues that creating someone who will disbelieve is not foolish, so willing their disbelief is also not foolish.
C) He denies that God knows future disbelief.
D) He says that God only wills belief, not disbelief.

Key Takeaway
God's will is not subject to human moral evaluation; His actions are beyond foolishness.

God's Absolute Justice and the Infliction of Pain on Children
Ash'ari affirms God's absolute justice even if He inflicts pain on children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Ash'ari, God's justice is not measured by human standards, and that His actions are just by virtue of being His, while His actual treatment of believers and disbelievers is based on His truthful revelation.

Core Idea
God's justice is defined by His will, not by human concepts of fairness.

This lesson addresses a challenging question: can God inflict pain on children in the afterlife? The Ash'ari response may surprise you. Imam al-Ash'ari argues that God has the absolute right to do so, and that it would be just. However, He does not actually do it because He has told us otherwise. This teaches us that God's actions are not bound by our sense of right and wrong, but by His own nature and word.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the question-and-answer format. Ash'ari is defending God's absolute sovereignty.
The passage uses the term 'قبيح' (foul) to describe actions that are morally repugnant to us.
2
Identify the main claim
Ash'ari affirms that God can inflict pain on children and that it would be just. Find where he says 'وهو عادل إن فعلَه'.
Justice here is ascribed to God regardless of the act.
3
Examine the examples
The passage lists several divine actions that might seem unjust to humans. List them and see how they are all declared just.
Examples include unending punishment for a finite sin, subjugating animals, and favoring some over others.
4
Understand the limiting factor
Ash'ari says God does not actually do these things because He informed us otherwise. Note how the only limitation is God's own truthful report.
This emphasizes that God's action is constrained only by His own promise, not by an external standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣٩- مسألة [في إيلام الأطفال] فإن قال قائل : هلْ للَّه تَعَالَى - أن يُؤلم الأطفال في الآخرة؟ قيل له : لله - تعالى ذلك ؛ وهو عادل إنْ فَعَلَه ، وكذلك كلُّ مَا يَفعَلُه على جُرْمٍ متناءٍ بِعِقَابِ لَا يَتَنَاهَى ، وتَسْخِيرُ الحيوانِ بَعْضِهِم لِبَعْضٍ، والإنعامُ على بعضهم دونَ بعض، وخَلْقُه إيَّاهُم معَ عِلْمِه بأنَّهم يكفرونَ، كُلُّ ذلك عَدْلٌ مِنْهُ . وَلَا يَقْبُحُ مِنَ الله لَوِ ابْتَدَأَهُمْ بالعَذَابِ الأليمِ وأدَامَه ، ولا يقبحُ منه أنْ يُعذِّبَ المؤمنينَ ، ويُدْخِلَ الكافرينَ الجنان، وإِنَّما نقول : إنَّه لا يَفْعَلُ ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّه أَخْبَرَنَا : أَنَّه يُعاقِبُ الكافرين، وهُوَ لا يجوز عليه الكَذِبُ في خَبَرِه
Issue 39: On the infliction of pain upon children. If someone says: Does God - the Exalted - have the [right] to inflict pain on children in the afterlife? It is said to him: God - the Exalted - has that [right]; and He is just if He does it. Likewise, everything He does: punishing a sin with unending punishment, subjugating some animals to others, bestowing favors upon some to the exclusion of others, and creating them while knowing that they will disbelieve - all of that is justice from Him. And it is not foul from God if He were to begin [inflicting] painful punishment upon them and continue it, nor is it foul from Him if He were to punish the believers and admit the disbelievers into Paradise. We only say that He does not do that because He has informed us that He punishes the disbelievers, and lying in His report is not permissible for Him.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer format addressing the issue of whether God can inflict pain on children in the afterlife. The argument is a defense of God's absolute justice and sovereignty.

1. The question: 'Does God have the right to inflict pain on children in the afterlife?'
2. The answer: Yes, God has that right, and if He does it, He is just. This establishes that God's actions are not subject to human evaluation of justice; whatever He does is just by definition.
3. The author then lists several examples of God's actions that might appear unjust from a human perspective: punishing a sin endlessly, subjugating animals to one another, favoring some over others, and creating people knowing they will disbelieve. Yet all are just because they come from God.
4. The author goes further: It would not be foul (قبيح) for God to begin punishing people with painful torment and continue it, nor would it be foul for Him to punish believers and admit disbelievers into Paradise. This emphasizes that God's actions are not bound by human concepts of fairness.
5. The reason God does not actually do these things is not because they are inherently unjust, but because He has informed us through revelation that He punishes disbelievers and rewards believers. Since God cannot lie, His promise and threat are true, and He acts accordingly.

Thus, the passage upholds the Ash'ari principle that God's actions are just by His decree, not measured by an independent standard. The only constraint on God's action is His own word, which is truthful.

Key Terms
إيلام
أطفال
عدل
قبح
عذاب
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's justice requires Him to treat all beings equally or that He cannot inflict pain on the innocent.
Correction: Ash'ari teaches that God's actions are just by definition, and He has the right to inflict pain on anyone, but He does not do so arbitrarily because He has promised otherwise in His truthful revelation.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, why does God not actually inflict pain on children in the afterlife?
A) Because it would be unjust.
B) Because He has informed us that He punishes disbelievers and rewards believers, and He does not lie.
C) Because children are sinless and deserve reward.
D) Because inflicting pain is inherently evil.

Key Takeaway
God's justice is absolute and not bound by human standards; He does what He wills, but His actual treatment of creation aligns with His truthful revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a tragedy that seems utterly undeserved, do you secretly resent God for being 'unfair'? How does Ash'ari's claim that 'whatever God does is just' challenge that resentment, and what would it cost you to truly accept it?

?
Ash'ari says it would not be foul for God to reward disbelievers and punish believers. Do you have any hidden assumption that your own faith or good deeds obligate God to treat you well? How does this passage expose that assumption, and what concrete change in your attitude toward God's mercy does it demand?

The Divine Origin of Moral Values
Al-Ash'ari explains that good and evil are based on divine command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari principle that moral values like good and evil are determined solely by God's command, not by inherent qualities.

Core Idea
Nothing is good or evil until God declares it so.

This lesson introduces a foundational Ash'ari position on the origin of moral values. Al-Ash'ari responds to an opponent who claims lying is evil because of its inherent nature. Instead, he argues that God's declaration makes it evil, and if He declared it good, it would be good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing how al-Ash'ari responds to the objection. Focus on the words 'قبحه', 'حسنه', and 'أمر'.
The phrase 'العقليين' is ambiguous; it likely refers to rational beings or reason.
2
Identify the objection and response
The opponent says lying is evil because God declared it evil. Al-Ash'ari agrees and adds that if God declared it good, it would be good. This shows that moral values depend on divine command.
3
Reflect on divine omnipotence
Consider the implication: God has absolute authority over moral categories. There is no independent standard of good and evil outside His will.
This is a key Ash'ari doctrine opposing Mu'tazilite rationalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التحسين والتقبيح فإن قال: فإنما يقبح الكذب؛ لأنه قبحه. قيل له: أجل، ولو حسنه لكان حسنًا. ولو أمر به لم يكن عليه العقليين. اعتراض. فإن قالوا: فجوزوا عليه أن يكذب كما جوزتم أن يأمر بالكذب؟ قيل لهم: ليس كل ما جاز أن يأمر به جاز أن يوصف به.
On declaring good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih). Then if he says: Lying is only evil because He declared it evil. It is said to him: Yes, and if He had declared it good, it would be good. And if He commanded it, there would be no [judgment] upon the two rational [beings?]. Objection. Then if they say: Then allow that He could lie, just as you allowed that He could command lying? It is said to them: Not everything that is permissible for Him to command is permissible for Him to be described by.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage dealing with the Ash'ari theory of al-tahsin wa al-taqbih (declaring good and evil). The first objection is from an opponent who says that lying is only evil because God declared it evil. Al-Ash'ari agrees, adding that if God had declared it good, it would be good, and if He commanded it, there would be no rational judgment against Him (the phrase 'العقليين' is ambiguous but likely refers to rational beings or reason). Then a second objection follows: if God can command lying, then He could also lie. Al-Ash'ari distinguishes between commanding an act and being described by that act: not everything permissible for God to command is permissible for Him to be described by. Thus, God can command lying hypothetically, but He cannot be described as lying because truthfulness is essential to His attributes. This distinction preserves God's necessary truthfulness while affirming His absolute authority over moral judgments.

Key Terms

تقبيح
كذب
أمر
عقليين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Its inherent nature
B) God's declaration
C) Human reason
D) Social consequences

Key Takeaway
Moral values are entirely dependent on God's command; there is no independent standard of good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to submit your entire ethical framework to God, even if it contradicts your deepest convictions? Write a specific area where you struggle to submit and resolve to surrender it today.

Distinguishing Command from Attribute: God's Truthfulness
Al-Ash'ari distinguishes between commanding an act and being described by it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God commanding an act and God being described by that act, and why this preserves His truthfulness.

Core Idea
God can command what He cannot be described by.

Building on the previous lesson, a new objection arises: if God can command lying, then He could also lie. Al-Ash'ari responds with a crucial distinction between commanding an act and having that act as an attribute. This lesson explores that distinction and its significance for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the objection uses the logic from the previous lesson to argue that God could lie.
2
Identify the key distinction
Al-Ash'ari says: 'Not everything that is permissible for Him to command is permissible for Him to be described by.' This is the core of his answer.
The word 'يوصف' (be described) is key.
3
Apply the distinction
Think of an example: God can command theft, but He cannot be described as a thief. Similarly, He can command lying hypothetically, but He cannot be described as a liar.
God's attributes (like truthfulness) are necessary and unchanging.

The Evilness of Lying Depends on God's Command
Ashari handles the objection that lying is evil by linking its evilness to God's command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ashari argues that moral values like evilness depend on God's command, and that there is a distinction between what God can command and what He can be described as doing.

Core Idea
Evil is not intrinsic; it depends on God’s command.

This brief passage presents a dialectical exchange: an objector claims lying is evil because God declared it so. Ashari agrees but draws a crucial distinction between commanding an action and being described by it. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the dialogue format: objection followed by response.
2
Identify the two objections and responses
Label clearly: the first objection says lying is evil because God declared it evil; Ashari agrees but adds that if God declared it good it would be good. The second objection says if commanding lying is permissible, why shouldn't lying itself be permissible? Ashari distinguishes between commanding and being described with an attribute.
This shows the logical progression of the debate.
3
Reflect on the key distinction
Think about why commanding an act is different from being described by it. For example, a judge can command a punishment but is not himself a criminal. Similarly, God's command does not imply He possesses the attribute.
This upholds God's perfection while affirming His authority over moral values.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can identify Ashari's answer to the second objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: فإنما يقبح الكذب؛ لأنه قبحه. قيل له: أجل، ولو حسنه لكان حسنا. ولو أمر به لم يكن عليه العقليين. فإن قالوا: فجوزوا عليه أن يكذب كما جوزتم أن يأمر بالكذب؟ قيل لهم: ليس كل ما جاز أن يأمر به جاز أن يوصف به.
If he says: 'Lying is only evil because He declared it evil.' It is said to him: 'Yes, and if He declared it good, it would be good. And if He commanded it, there would be no rational [obligation] upon Him.' If they say: 'Then allow Him to lie just as you allowed Him to command lying?' It is said to them: 'Not everything that it is permissible for Him to command is permissible to be described with.'
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer. First, the objector claims that lying is evil because God declared it evil. Ashari agrees but adds: if God had declared it good, it would be good. The evilness is not intrinsic but based on divine declaration. Similarly, if God commanded lying, there would be no rational obligation preventing it. Then a further objection: if commanding lying is permissible, why not lying itself? The answer: there is a distinction between what God can command and what He can be described as doing. Not everything permissible to command is permissible to attribute to God.

Key Terms
الكذب
التقبيح
الأمر
الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God can command lying, then He can lie.
Correction: Ashari distinguishes between commanding an action and being described by it. Commanding lying does not entail that God lies; it only shows that moral values are not intrinsic.

Checkpoint
How does Ashari respond to the objection that if God can command lying, He can also lie?
A) He says lying is impossible for God due to His essence
B) He says God never commands lying
C) He says not everything God can command can be attributed to Him
D) He says lying is not evil if commanded

Key Takeaway
Moral evilness is not an intrinsic property; it depends on God's command, and God's perfection prevents Him from being described with evil even if He could command it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you condemn something as evil, do you truly submit to God's authority as the sole arbiter of good and evil, or do you rely on your own sense of right and wrong? How can you align your moral judgments with this teaching?
?
Reflect on a time you were certain an action was evil. Did you consider whether God has commanded it? If not, what does that say about where your moral compass truly points?
?
If moral values depend entirely on God's command, is there any action you would find difficult to accept as good if God commanded it? What does your resistance reveal about your heart's attachment to your own moral framework?

The Analogy of Divine Commands: Why Lying Is Impossible for God
Ashari explains the impossibility of lying using an analogy with commanded actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy Ashari uses to show that lying is impossible for God, and why the impossibility is not based on the evilness of lying but on divine transcendence.

Core Idea
What God commands for us is not necessarily possible for Him.

Ashari begins by drawing a parallel between physical actions and lying. Just as God commands us to pray, humble ourselves, and move, yet these actions are impossible for Him due to His transcendence, so lying is impossible for God. This analogy sets the stage for a deeper argument about essential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note how Ashari compares divine commands to divine impossibility.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ليس لقبحه' (not because of its evilness).
2
Identify the analogy
Find the actions God commands and see why they are impossible for Him. Then see how lying fits the same pattern.
3
Understand 'not because of evilness'
Ashari stresses that the reason lying is impossible is not moral but ontological. Reflect on this distinction.
This is a key point in kalam theology.
4
Summarize the argument
In your own words, explain why Ashari uses this analogy to prove the impossibility of lying for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألا ترون أنه قد أمرنا أن نصلي، ونخضع، ونتحرك، ولا يجوز عليه أن يصلي، ويخضع، ويتحرك؛ لأن ذلك مستحيل عليه؟ وكذلك لا يجوز عليه الكذب، ليس لقبحه، ولكن لأنه يستحيل عليه الكذب، ولا يجوز أن يوصف بالقدرة على أن يكذب، كما لا يجوز وصفه بالقدرة على أن يتحرك ويجهل. ولو جاز لزاعم أن يزعم أنه يوصف الباري بالقدرة على أن يكذب، ولا يوصف بالقدرة على أن يجهل، ولا يأتي بين ذلك بوجود الجهل له، كذلك الصدق من صفات ذاته، ويستحيل عليه العدم... ويتضاد أن يكون صادقًا كاذبًا في شيء وشيئين، كما يستحيل أن يكون عالمًا وجاهلاً في شيء وشيئين، ومن وجه ووجهين.
Do you not see that He commanded us to pray, to humble ourselves, and to move, but it is not permissible for Him to pray, to humble Himself, and to move; because that is impossible for Him? Similarly, lying is not permissible for Him, not because of its evilness, but because lying is impossible for Him, and He cannot be described as having power to lie, just as He cannot be described as having power to move and to be ignorant. And if a claimant should claim that the Creator is described as having power to lie, but not described as having power to be ignorant, and no difference comes between that through the existence of ignorance for Him, likewise truthfulness is among the attributes of His essence, and its absence is impossible for Him... And it is contradictory that He be truthful and lying in one thing or two things, just as it is impossible that He be knowing and ignorant in one thing or two things, and from one aspect or two aspects.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage uses an analogy to prove that lying is impossible for God. God commands us to perform actions like prayer, humility, and movement, but He Himself cannot do these because they are impossible for His essence. Similarly, lying is impossible for God, not because it is evil (قبيح), but because truthfulness is an essential attribute of His essence (صفات الذات). Just as ignorance is impossible for God because knowledge is essential, lying is impossible because truthfulness is essential. The passage also rejects the idea that God could have power to lie, since that would imply a potential for falsehood, which is incompatible with His essential truthfulness. Finally, it argues that essential attributes cannot be contradictory; God cannot be truthful and lying at the same time or in different matters, just as He cannot be knowing and ignorant.

Key Terms
الكذب
الاستحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot lie because lying is evil.
Correction: Ashari explicitly states the impossibility is not due to evilness (قبح) but because lying is impossible for God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to Ashari, why is lying impossible for God?
A) Because lying is morally evil
B) Because God cannot command it
C) Because it is impossible for Him, just like movement and ignorance
D) Because God has no power to do anything

Key Takeaway
Lying is impossible for God because it is an action He cannot perform, not because it is morally wrong.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If lying is impossible for God because it contradicts His nature, what does your own habit of lying reveal about your nature? Are you living out the truth of who you were created to be, or have you made peace with a contradiction?
?
God commands you to humble yourself and move in worship, yet these very acts are impossible for Him. How does this reality reshape your understanding of His transcendence and your complete dependence on Him? In what area of your life are you acting as if God owes you an explanation?
?
This lesson says God cannot even be described as having the power to lie. How does that rock your trust in His promises? Is there any promise of God you doubt? What would it take for you to root that doubt out of your heart today?

Essential Attributes: Why Truthfulness Precludes Lying
Ashari grounds the impossibility of lying in truthfulness as an essential attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that truthfulness is an essential attribute of God, making lying impossible in all circumstances.

Core Idea
God's essential attributes cannot be contradicted.

In this continuation of his argument, Ashari deepens the analysis. He imagines a claimant who might allow God the power to lie but not the power to be ignorant. Ashari shows this is inconsistent because truthfulness, like knowledge, is an essential attribute. Thus lying is not merely impossible; it is ontologically excluded.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Note the hypothetical claim and Ashari's response.
2
Identify the hypothetical claim
Find the claim that God could have power to lie but not power to be ignorant.
This is a imagined objection.
3
Understand the concept of essential attributes
Ashari states that truthfulness is from God's essential attributes. Understand what that means.
Essential attributes (صفات الذات) are those that are permanently inherent in God.
4
Reflect on the contradiction
Why is it contradictory for God to be truthful and lying, just as it is contradictory to be knowing and ignorant?
This shows that essential attributes exclude their opposites.

Distinction Between Commanding and Being Described
Ashari answers the objection about God and lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ashari argues that what God may command does not necessarily entail that He be described by that same action.

Core Idea
God's commands are for creatures, not for Himself.

A common objection against Ashari's position is that if God can command lying, then He could also lie. Ashari's response introduces a critical distinction: commanding an action is not the same as being described by that action. In this passage, he provides clear examples to illustrate that God commands actions He never performs, such as movement and obedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the rhetorical question and the examples.
The passage is a response to an objection.
2
Identify the objection
Identify the opponent's argument: if God can command lying, He must be able to lie. Pinpoint where this is stated.
3
Analyze Ashari's distinction
Ashari distinguishes between commanding (al-amr) and being described (al-wasf). Understand that the two are not equivalent: commanding does not imply performing.
This is the key rational move.
4
Examine the examples
Reflect on the examples of movement (al-ḥaraka) and obedience (al-ṭā‘a). God commands both but does not move or obey, showing the distinction holds for other actions too.
Notice the logic: if it holds for these, it holds for lying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان يقول: إذا قيل له: إذا جوزت أن يأمر بالكذب فجوز أن يكذب: إن ذلك لأجل أنه ليس كل ما يجوز أن يأمر به يجوز أن يوصف به. ألا ترى أنه إذا أمر بالحركة فلا يجوز أن يتحرك، وأمر بالطاعة ولا يجوز أن يطيع.
And he used to say: When it is said to him: 'Since you allow Him to command lying, allow Him to lie.' [He replies:] 'That is because not everything that is permissible for Him to command is permissible to be described with. Do you not see that when He commands movement, He cannot move; and He commands obedience, but He cannot obey?'
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage provides a concise answer to the recurring objection: if it is permissible for God to command lying, why is it not permissible for Him to lie? Ashari's distinction is based on the fact that commanding an action does not entail that the commander performs that action. For example, God commands us to move, but He Himself does not move because movement is impossible for Him. Similarly, He commands obedience, but He cannot obey because obedience implies a superior. Thus, commanding lying is not the same as lying. Lying is impossible for God due to His essence, while commanding it is not impossible.

Key Terms
الأمر
الوصف
الحركة
الطاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God can command lying, He must be capable of lying; otherwise there is a contradiction.
Correction: Commanding an action does not entail performing it, as shown by God commanding movement and obedience without doing either.

Checkpoint
What example does Ashari give to show that commanding an action does not imply performing it?
A) God commands prayer but does not pray
B) God commands lying but does not lie
C) God commands movement but does not move
D) God commands knowledge but does not know

Key Takeaway
God's command does not imply His own performance; the two categories are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that God is perfect and does not lie. But have you ever justified a small lie to yourself, thinking it is harmless? How does this lesson's distinction between commanding and being described challenge your willingness to excuse yourself while affirming God's perfection?
?
If God commands obedience but does not obey, He is the ultimate authority. Where in your life do you still act as if you are the final judge of what is right, rather than submitting to His commands?
?
The objection in this lesson assumes consistency between what God commands and what He does. When you look at your own life, do you hold yourself to the same standards you demand of others, or do you conveniently separate your commands from your actions like the objector falsely claimed about God?

Why Lying is Impossible for God
Al-Ash'ari establishes the impossibility of lying for God and refutes a symmetrical claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari argues that lying is impossible for God, not because of its ugliness but because of its inherent impossibility, and how he refutes the opposing view that God could have power to lie.

Core Idea
Lying is impossible for God not because it is evil, but because it is impossible for His nature.

Al-Ash'ari begins by drawing an analogy from acts God commands us to do but cannot do Himself, such as prayer and movement. He then applies the same reasoning to lying, affirming that God cannot lie – not because lying is evil, but because it is impossible for Him. He also refutes an objection that attempts to attribute power to lie to God while denying power to be ignorant.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Notice how al-Ash'ari uses the example of prayer, humility, and movement to establish a pattern: God commands them but cannot do them. Then he applies the same logic to lying.
2
Identify the key distinction
Al-Ash'ari explicitly says lying is impossible not because of its ugliness but because of impossibility. Mark this phrase.
The Arabic: ليس لقُبْحِه، ولَكِنْ لأَنَّه يَسْتحيلُ عليه الكَذِبُ
3
Follow the refutation
The symmetry argument shows that if one could claim God has power to lie but not power to be ignorant without proof, then one could reverse it, leading to absurdity. Understand why this refutes the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في التعديل والتَّجْويرِ. أَلَا تَرَوْنَ أَنَّه قَدْ أَمرنا أن نُصلِّي، وَنَحْضَعَ، ونَتَحرَّكَ، ولا يَجُوزُ عليه أن يُصلِّي، ويَخْضَعَ، ويتحرَّك؛ لأنَّ ذلك مستحيل عَلَيْهِ؟ وكذلِكَ لا يجوز عليه الكَذِبُ، ليس لقُبْحِه، ولَكِنْ لأَنَّه يَسْتحيلُ عليه الكَذِبُ، ولا يَجوزُ أن يُوصَفَ بالقُدْرَةِ على أن يَكْذِبَ، كَما لا يَجوز وصفه بالقُدرةِ على أن يتحرَّكَ ويَجْهَلَ. ولَوْ جَازَ لزاعِمٍ أَنْ يَزْعُمَ أَنَّه يُوصَفُ البارِي بالقُدْرَةِ على أَنْ يَكْذِبَ، ولا يُوصَفُ بالقُدْرَةِ على أنْ يَجْهَلَ، وَلَا يَأْتِي بَيْنَ ذلك بفُرْقَانٍ، لجَازَ لِقَالِب أن يَقْلِبَ القِصَّة، فَيَزْعُمَ أن الباري يُوصَفُ بالقُدْرَةِ عَلَى أَنْ يَجْهَلَ، ولا يُوصَفُ بالقُدْرَةِ على أن يَكْذِبَ. فلمَّا لم يَجُز ذلكَ بَطَلَ ما قالُوه. فأما الأمر بالكذب وجواز ورودِه بذلك. فإنَّه كان يقولُ: «لَوْ وَرَدَ الأمرُ به لم يَسْتَحِلْ، مع أنَّه قد رُوِيَ أَنَّ الله -تعالى- أباحَ الكَذِبَ في ثلاثة مواضع، ونَهى عَنِ الصِّدقِ في كثير من المواضِعِ، فَقَبُحَ صِدْقٌ لأجل النَّهي، وحَسُنَ كَذِبٌ لأجلِ الأمرِ. فعُلِمَ أنَّ ذلك لم يَقْبُحْ وَلَمْ يَحْسُنُ لأَجْلِ أنَّه صِدْقٌ وكَذِبٌ. وكان يقولُ: إذا قيل له: إذا جَوَّزْتَ أن يَأْمُرَ بالكَذِبِ فَجَوِّزُ أن يَكْذِبَ: إنَّ ذلكَ لأجلِ أنَّه ليسَ كُلُّ ما يجوز أن يأمُرَ به يَجُوزُ أَن يُوصَفَ به. أَلَا تَرَى أنَّه إذا أَمَرَ بالحَرَكَةِ فلا يَجُوزُ أن يتحرَّكَ، وأمرَ بالطَّاعَةِ ولا يَجوزُ أَن يُطِيعَ»
Chapter on Justification and Injustice. Do you not see that He commanded us to pray, to humble ourselves, and to move, and it is not permissible for Him to pray, to humble Himself, or to move, because that is impossible for Him? And similarly, lying is not permissible for Him, not because of its ugliness, but because lying is impossible for Him, and it is not permissible to describe Him as having power to lie, just as it is not permissible to describe Him as having power to move and to be ignorant. And if a claimant were to claim that the Creator can be described as having power to lie but not described as having power to be ignorant, and he brings no distinguishing proof between them, then it would be permissible for a turner to reverse the story and claim that the Creator can be described as having power to be ignorant but not described as having power to lie. Since that is not permissible, their statement is void. As for the command to lie and the possibility of it occurring, he used to say: 'If the command to lie came, it would not be impossible, and indeed it has been reported that God - exalted - permitted lying in three situations and forbade truthfulness in many situations, so truth became ugly because of the prohibition and lying became good because of the command. So it was known that it is not ugly or good because it is truth or lying.' And he used to say, when it was said to him: 'If you permit the command to lie, then permit that He lie': 'That is because not everything that He may command is permissible to attribute to Him. Do you not see that when He commands movement, He cannot move, and He commands obedience, yet He cannot obey.'
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. The author begins by establishing a principle: God commands us to perform acts like prayer, humility, and movement, but we cannot attribute these acts to God because they are impossible for Him. Similarly, lying is impossible for God, not because it is inherently wrong, but because it is inherently impossible for Him. Therefore, it is not correct to describe God as having power to lie. The author then addresses an opponent who claims that God can be described as having power to lie but not as having power to be ignorant, without providing a distinguishing proof. The author refutes this by showing that if that were allowed, one could reverse the claim and attribute ignorance to God instead, which is absurd. Hence the opponent's argument collapses. Next, the author presents a quote from an opponent who argues that if God commanded lying, it would be good, and that lying is permitted in some cases according to reports, so the moral value of lying depends on divine command, not on its intrinsic nature. The opponent also distinguishes between commanding an act and performing it: God can command movement without moving, and command obedience without obeying. The author records this view as part of the discussion, but the overall argument affirms that God's attributes are unique and that He cannot be described with imperfections.

Key Terms
الكذب
الصدق
القدرة
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot lie because lying is inherently evil.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari explicitly states it is not because of ugliness but because lying is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, why is lying impossible for God?
A) Because lying is evil.
B) Because God lacks the power to lie.
C) Because lying is inherently impossible for God, not because of its ugliness.
D) Because God commands truth.

Key Takeaway
Lying is impossible for God because it contradicts His nature, not because it is morally wrong.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot lie because it is impossible for Him, how does that affect your trust in His promises? Is there a specific promise from the Quran that you struggle to believe? What will you do this week to act on that promise with certainty?
?
The passage refutes the idea that God could lie but not be ignorant. Are there times when you try to fit God into your own moral framework, thinking He 'should' or 'should not' act a certain way? How can you submit to His transcendence and let Him define what is possible?
?
God's inability to lie means He is bound by His own nature, not by outside rules. Does this make you feel more secure or more challenged in your relationship with Him? What aspect of God's nature do you need to trust more deeply today?

Can God Command Lying? The Distinction Between Command and Attribute
Al-Ash'ari presents and refutes the view that God could command lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that if God commanded lying it would be good, and al-Ash'ari's refutation through the distinction between what God commands and what He does.

Core Idea
What God commands does not necessarily reflect what He does; He can command what is impossible for Him.

The passage continues with a quote from an opponent who argued that if God commanded lying, it would become good, and that God can command something without doing it Himself. Al-Ash'ari records this view as part of the discussion, demonstrating the nuances of divine command and attribute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says if God commanded lying, it would not be impossible, and there are reports of God permitting lying in certain situations.
2
Understand the moral implication
The opponent argues that truth and lying are not intrinsically good or bad; their value depends on divine command.
3
Grasp al-Ash'ari's response
Al-Ash'ari points out that not everything God can command can be attributed to Him, using the examples of movement and obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في التعديل والتَّجْويرِ. أَلَا تَرَوْنَ أَنَّه قَدْ أَمرنا أن نُصلِّي، وَنَحْضَعَ، ونَتَحرَّكَ، ولا يَجُوزُ عليه أن يُصلِّي، ويَخْضَعَ، ويتحرَّك؛ لأنَّ ذلك مستحيل عَلَيْهِ؟ وكذلِكَ لا يجوز عليه الكَذِبُ، ليس لقُبْحِه، ولَكِنْ لأَنَّه يَسْتحيلُ عليه الكَذِبُ، ولا يَجوزُ أن يُوصَفَ بالقُدْرَةِ على أن يَكْذِبَ، كَما لا يَجوز وصفه بالقُدرةِ على أن يتحرَّكَ ويَجْهَلَ. ولَوْ جَازَ لزاعِمٍ أَنْ يَزْعُمَ أَنَّه يُوصَفُ البارِي بالقُدْرَةِ على أَنْ يَكْذِبَ، ولا يُوصَفُ بالقُدْرَةِ على أنْ يَجْهَلَ، وَلَا يَأْتِي بَيْنَ ذلك بفُرْقَانٍ، لجَازَ لِقَالِب أن يَقْلِبَ القِصَّة، فَيَزْعُمَ أن الباري يُوصَفُ بالقُدْرَةِ عَلَى أَنْ يَجْهَلَ، ولا يُوصَفُ بالقُدْرَةِ على أن يَكْذِبَ. فلمَّا لم يَجُز ذلكَ بَطَلَ ما قالُوه. فأما الأمر بالكذب وجواز ورودِه بذلك. فإنَّه كان يقولُ: «لَوْ وَرَدَ الأمرُ به لم يَسْتَحِلْ، مع أنَّه قد رُوِيَ أَنَّ الله -تعالى- أباحَ الكَذِبَ في ثلاثة مواضع، ونَهى عَنِ الصِّدقِ في كثير من المواضِعِ، فَقَبُحَ صِدْقٌ لأجل النَّهي، وحَسُنَ كَذِبٌ لأجلِ الأمرِ. فعُلِمَ أنَّ ذلك لم يَقْبُحْ وَلَمْ يَحْسُنُ لأَجْلِ أنَّه صِدْقٌ وكَذِبٌ. وكان يقولُ: إذا قيل له: إذا جَوَّزْتَ أن يَأْمُرَ بالكَذِبِ فَجَوِّزُ أن يَكْذِبَ: إنَّ ذلكَ لأجلِ أنَّه ليسَ كُلُّ ما يجوز أن يأمُرَ به يَجُوزُ أَن يُوصَفَ به. أَلَا تَرَى أنَّه إذا أَمَرَ بالحَرَكَةِ فلا يَجُوزُ أن يتحرَّكَ، وأمرَ بالطَّاعَةِ ولا يَجوزُ أَن يُطِيعَ»
Chapter on Justification and Injustice. Do you not see that He commanded us to pray, to humble ourselves, and to move, and it is not permissible for Him to pray, to humble Himself, or to move, because that is impossible for Him? And similarly, lying is not permissible for Him, not because of its ugliness, but because lying is impossible for Him, and it is not permissible to describe Him as having power to lie, just as it is not permissible to describe Him as having power to move and to be ignorant. And if a claimant were to claim that the Creator can be described as having power to lie but not described as having power to be ignorant, and he brings no distinguishing proof between them, then it would be permissible for a turner to reverse the story and claim that the Creator can be described as having power to be ignorant but not described as having power to lie. Since that is not permissible, their statement is void. As for the command to lie and the possibility of it occurring, he used to say: 'If the command to lie came, it would not be impossible, and indeed it has been reported that God - exalted - permitted lying in three situations and forbade truthfulness in many situations, so truth became ugly because of the prohibition and lying became good because of the command. So it was known that it is not ugly or good because it is truth or lying.' And he used to say, when it was said to him: 'If you permit the command to lie, then permit that He lie': 'That is because not everything that He may command is permissible to attribute to Him. Do you not see that when He commands movement, He cannot move, and He commands obedience, yet He cannot obey.'
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. The author begins by establishing a principle: God commands us to perform acts like prayer, humility, and movement, but we cannot attribute these acts to God because they are impossible for Him. Similarly, lying is impossible for God, not because it is inherently wrong, but because it is inherently impossible for Him. Therefore, it is not correct to describe God as having power to lie. The author then addresses an opponent who claims that God can be described as having power to lie but not as having power to be ignorant, without providing a distinguishing proof. The author refutes this by showing that if that were allowed, one could reverse the claim and attribute ignorance to God instead, which is absurd. Hence the opponent's argument collapses. Next, the author presents a quote from an opponent who argues that if God commanded lying, it would be good, and that lying is permitted in some cases according to reports, so the moral value of lying depends on divine command, not on its intrinsic nature. The opponent also distinguishes between commanding an act and performing it: God can command movement without moving, and command obedience without obeying. The author records this view as part of the discussion, but the overall argument affirms that God's attributes are unique and that He cannot be described with imperfections.

Key Terms

الصدق
الأمر
الحركة
الطاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God commands an action, He must be capable of performing it Himself.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that God commands movement and obedience but cannot move or obey, so the same applies to lying.

Checkpoint
What example does al-Ash'ari use to show that God can command something He cannot do?
A) Prayer and humility.
B) Movement and obedience.
C) Truth and lying.
D) Knowledge and ignorance.

Key Takeaway
God can command actions He does not Himself perform, so the possibility of commanding lying does not imply God can lie.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God commanded something you deeply believe is wrong, would you still see it as good? How does this passage challenge you to submit your moral compass entirely to divine revelation?
?
Consider a time you judged an action as good or evil based on its outcome rather than God's command. How can you train yourself to view moral value as determined solely by His decree, even when it feels uncomfortable?

The Principle of Command and Attribution
Al-Ash'ari answers an objection about God commanding prayer while being free from movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari refutes the objection that commanding an action implies the commander is described by that action.

Core Idea
Commanding an act does not make the commander share in that act's nature.

Al-Ash'ari presents an objection from an opponent who argues that since God commands prayer (which involves movement), movement must inhere in God. The opponent tries to distinguish between acts that inhere and acts performed. Al-Ash'ari responds by showing the logical flaw: if commanding were enough to attribute the act to the commander, then God would be attributed with everything He commands. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opponent's argument: God commands prayer, prayer involves movements, so movement inheres in the worshipper. The opponent contrasts this with insult and lying, which are performed but not inhering. Why does he make this distinction?
2
Identify the opponent's logical leap
The opponent implies that because God commands prayer, He must be subject to movement. Note how he tries to separate acts of command from acts of performance.
The opponent's real target is to force an anthropomorphic conclusion.
3
Read Al-Ash'ari's response
Al-Ash'ari points out the inconsistency: if the reason for attributing lying to God is that He commanded it, then by the same logic every commanded act would be attributable to Him, leading to absurdity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Understand the refuted principle
The refutation shows that commanding an act does not necessitate that the commander is described by that act. The opponent's analogy fails because command and performance are distinct.
This is a key principle in divine attributes.
5
Apply the principle
Consider other divine commands (e.g., to give charity, to be just). Does commanding these acts imply Allah is described by them? Reflect on why the answer is no.
This shields divine transcendence from anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : إذا أَمَرَ اللَّهُ -تعالى- أن نُصَلِّي فَصَلَاتنا هِيَ حَرَكاتُنا، التي نتحرَّكُ إذا صَلَّيْنا ، والمُتحرِّكُ مُتحرِّك لحلولِ الحركة فيه، والشاتِمُ والكَاذِبُ إنَّما كان شاتما كاذبا ؛ لأنَّه فَعَلَ الشَّتْمَ ، والكَذِبَ، لا لأنَّ ذلك حَلَّ فِيهِ . يُقال له : إنْ كانَتِ العلةُ التي لها ألزمنا أن نُجَوِّزَ أَنْ يَكْذِبَ إلزامهم بمقالة أهل الباري - تعالى عن ذلك علوا كبيرًا - : أنه أمر به، فيَجِبُ في كلِّ شيءٍ أمرَ به أن يَجوزَ وَصفُه بِهِ، فإذَا أمرَ أنْ تَحِلَّ فِي أَنفُسِنا حَركات نتحرَّكُ بِهَا ، وَصَلَاة نُصلِّي بها ، لَزِمَ أَن يَجوزَ أَن تَحِلَّ فِي نَفْسِه حَرَكَاتُ يَتَحَرَّكُ بها ، وَصَلَاةٌ يُصلِّي بها
If someone says: When God - exalted - commands us to pray, our prayer is our movements by which we move when we pray, and a mover is a mover due to the inherence of movement in him, whereas an insulter and liar is an insulter and liar only because he performed insult and lying, not because that inhered in him. It is said to him: If the reason we required that He be considered able to lie is the imposition of the statement of the people of the Creator (exalted above that greatly) that He commanded it, then it would be necessary that for everything He commands, it be permissible to describe Him with it. Then if He commanded that movements by which we move and prayer by which we pray inhere in us, then it would be necessary that it be permissible for movements by which He moves and prayer by which He prays to inhere in Him.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues from the analogy of prayer: God commands us to perform prayer which consists of movements, and a mover is one in whom movement inheres. But an insulter or liar is considered such because they perform the act, not because the act inheres in them. The objector implies that attributing the act to God differently. The author responds by stating the underlying principle: if the necessity of attributing lying to God is based on the fact that He commanded it, then for any act He commands, it would be permissible to attribute that act to Him. So if He commanded movements and prayer to inhere in us, then it would follow that movements and prayer could inhere in Him as well. This shows the flaw in the objector's reasoning, as the command does not necessitate the attribute in the commander.

الشتم
الكذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that commanding an act implies the commander can perform it or is described by it.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that command and performance are distinct; commanding does not necessitate the attribute in the commander.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the author's response to the objector?
A) God cannot command what He Himself does not do.
B) Commanding an action does not mean the commander is described by that action.
C) Prayer involves inherence of movement in the worshipper, but not in God.
D) God can lie because He commanded it in some cases.

Key Takeaway
Commanding an action does not entail that the commander is described by that action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Objector's Distinction and Ash'ari's Conditional Argument
Imam Ash'ari presents an objection and responds with a conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ash'ari responds to an objection by arguing that if God commands an action, He can be described with that action, and the only way to avoid this is to adopt a distinction that leads to a valid question.

Core Idea
God's command implies His ability to be described with the commanded thing.

Imam Ash'ari presents an objection from his opponents who claim that prayer is merely a human movement inhering in us, not in God. He replies with a powerful conditional argument: if God commands something, then describing Him with that thing must be permissible. The only exception would be if the opponents admit that God can command a lie but not perform it, which then forces them to explain why He can't perform a lie to make another a liar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow.
Notice the objection starts with 'فإن قال قائل'.
2
Identify the objector's distinction
What distinction does the objector make between prayer (inherence) and lying/insulting (performance)?
He says prayer inheres in us, but lying is just an act we do.
3
Understand Ash'ari's conditional argument
Ash'ari argues that if the reason we allow God to lie is because He commands it, then for every command, describing God with that command is necessary.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Examine the exception
Ash'ari presents an exception the opponents might use: that God can command a lie but not do it Himself. He then asks why God cannot perform a lie that makes another a liar, just as He can perform prayer for another.
Ash'ari says this question is not objectionable, meaning it is a valid challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في التعديل والتجوير
بفرقان، لجاز لقالب أن يقلب القصة، فيزعم أن الباري يوصف بالقدرة على أن يجهل، ولا يوصف بالقدرة على أن يكذب. فلما لم يجز ذلك بطل ما قالوه.
فإن قال قائل: إذا أمر الله تعالى أن نصلي فصلاتنا هي حركاتنا، التي نتحرك إذا صلينا، والمتحرك متحرك لحلول الحركة فيه، والشاتم والكاذب إنما كان شاتما كاذبا؛ لأنه فعل الشتم، والكذب، لا لأن ذلك حل فيه.
يقال له: إن كانت العلة التي لها ألزمنا أن نجوز أن يكذب إلزامهم بمقالة أهل الباري - تعالى عن ذلك علوا كبيرا -: أنه أمر به، فيجب في كل شيء أمر به أن يجوز وصفه به، فإذا أمر أن تحل في أنفسنا حركات نتحرك بها، وصلاة نصلي بها، لزم أن يجوز أن تحل في نفسه حركات يتحرك بها، وصلاة يصلي بها، اللهم إلا أن يقولوا: إذا جاز أن يأمر الباري غيره بالكذب، فلم لا يجوز أن يفعل كذبا يكون به غيره كاذبا؟ كما إذا أمر غيره أن يصلي، جاز أن يفعل لغيره صلاة كان غيره بها مصليا. فإن سألونا عن هذا السؤال على هذا الوجه فهذا ما لا ينكر.
على أنه إن كان المصلي مصليا لحلول الصلاة فيه، كما أن المتحرك متحرك لحلول الحركة فيه، فواجب أن يكون كل جزء من الإنسان مصليا، كما كان كل جزء منه - إذا حلته الحركة - متحركا.
ويقال لهم: الصلاة في اللغة هي: الدعاء، فإن كان المصلي مصليا لحلول الصلاة فيه، فيجب أن يكون داعيا لحلول الدعاء فيه، وهذا فاسد عندهم.
ثم يقال لهم: إذا جاز أن يفعل الباري تعالى صلاة لغيره ويكون بها مصليا، فلم لا يفعل لغيره إرادة يكون بها مريدا، وكلاما يكون به متكلما؟
Chapter on Speech about Declaring Just and Unjust.
With a distinguishing criterion, it would be possible for an adversary to invert the story and claim that the Creator is described as having power to be ignorant, but is not described as having power to lie. But when that is not permissible, what they said is invalid.
If someone says: When God – exalted – commands us to pray, our prayer is our movements which we perform when we pray, and the mover is moved due to motion inhering in it, while the insulter and liar are only insulters and liars because they performed insult and lying, not because that inheres in them.
It is said to him: If the reason for which we obligated ourselves to allow that He can lie is the implication of the statement of the people of the Creator – exalted be He above that greatly – that He commanded it, then it must be for every thing He commands that it is permissible to describe Him with it. So when He commands that movements by which we move and prayer by which we pray inhere in ourselves, it follows that it is permissible that movements by which He moves and prayer by which He prays inhere in Himself, unless they say: Since it is permissible for the Creator to command another to lie, why is it not permissible for Him to perform a lie by which another becomes a liar? Just as when He commanded another to pray, it is permissible for Him to perform for another a prayer by which that other becomes a pray-er. If they ask us this question in this manner, this is not objectionable.
Moreover, if the pray-er is a pray-er due to the inherence of prayer in him, just as the mover is moved due to the inherence of motion in him, then it necessarily follows that every part of the human being is a pray-er, just as every part of him – when motion inheres in it – is moved.
And it is said to them: Prayer in language is supplication. If the pray-er is a pray-er due to the inherence of prayer in him, then it must be that he is a supplicant due to the inherence of supplication in him, and this is corrupt according to them.
Then it is said to them: If it is permissible for the Creator – exalted – to perform prayer for another and thereby be a pray-er, why does He not perform for another desire by which He would be desiring, and speech by which He would be speaking?
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument within the chapter on declaring things just and unjust. The author first sets up a hypothetical: if there were a distinguishing criterion (furqān) between cases, an opponent could invert the argument and claim that God can be described as capable of ignorance but not of lying. Since this is not valid, the opponent's initial claim is defeated.

Then an objector speaks: When God commands prayer, our prayer consists of our own movements, and the agent undergoing movement is moved because motion inheres in it. But the insulter and liar are so called because they perform the act, not because the act inheres in their essence. The objector implies that God's command does not necessitate that He be described with the commanded attribute.

The author responds: If the reason we were forced to allow that God could lie is because He commands it (as the opponents claimed), then for every thing He commands, it must be permissible to describe Him with that thing. Thus, if He commands movements and prayer to inhere in us, then it must be permissible for movements and prayer to inhere in Him. The author then adds an exception (illa an yaqūlū): if the opponents say that God can command another to lie but not Himself do it, then why can't He perform a lie that makes another a liar? Just as He can command another to pray and that other becomes a pray-er, so too could God perform a lie that makes another a liar. The author says this objection is not objectionable (mā lā yunkar), meaning it is a valid question that the opponents must answer.

Next, the author provides further refutations: First, if being a pray-er is due to the inherence of prayer (like motion), then every part of the human would be a pray-er, which is absurd. Second, the linguistic meaning of prayer is supplication; if inherence were the cause, then every part would be supplicating, which is false. Finally, the author extends the reasoning: if God can create prayer in another and thereby be a pray-er, why can't He create will or speech in another and thereby be described as willing or speaking? This forces the opponents to either accept the consequences or abandon their position.

Key Terms
تعديل وتجوير
فرقان
حلول
صلاة
كذب
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's command of an action has no bearing on whether He can be described with that action.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that the very act of commanding implies that the commander can be described with the commanded thing, otherwise the command would be incoherent.

Checkpoint
What is the logical consequence if we accept that God commanding something implies He can be described with it?
A) God must be capable of everything, even absurdities.
B) If God commands prayer, then prayer can inhere in Him.
C) God cannot command lies because that would describe Him with lies.
D) The objector's distinction is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
If God commands an action, He must be able to be described with that action, unless the opponents adopt a distinction that leads to further questioning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform salat, you are executing a command of Allah. Does this lesson challenge you to see His command as a reflection of His own attributes? How does that change the way you approach prayer?
?
You likely affirm that Allah cannot lie. But according to Ash'ari's conditional argument, if you hold that Allah's command is the reason for permitting description, then you must accept that Allah can be described with every commanded thing. Are you willing to follow this logic to its conclusion, or do you secretly rely on a distinction you cannot justify? Examine your own certainty.
?
The objector tried to separate God's actions from human actions by distinguishing inherence from performance. Does your own theology contain similar distinctions you have never questioned? What comfort or fear arises when you consider that God's commands might reveal something about His own nature?
